1. CURRENT SITUATION AND BACKGROUND
1.1   Civil war and unrest have plagued Sierra Leone for more than nine years. Since 1991,
successive governments have been unable to completely defeat the rebel forces of the
Revolutionary United Front (RUF), and a coup d'etat led by disgruntled junior anny officers in
May 1997 ousted the government of Ahmad Tejan Kabbah after only 14 months in office.
Though the President was reinstated to power in February 1998 by the regional military forces of
the Economic Community of West African States' Monitoring Group (ECOMOG), the security
situation deteriorated toward the end of the same year culminatg in a well-coordinated attack on
Freetown by RUF and former Sierra Leonean army forces in January 1999. While the attack on
Freetown was countered by ECOMOG forces, property damage and loss of life were high
particularly in the eastem part of the city, the large areas of the northern and eastern provinces of
the country fell under the control of rebels and former Sierra Leone army forces. In response,
diplomatic missions and nearly all international agencies pulled out of the country. Subsequent to
the atack, ECOMOG has slowly regained control over some of the territory that it had lost to the
rebels and a military stalemate was established.
1.2   The attack on Freetown was significant for two main reasons. First, it demonstrated that
the forces of the RUF and the former Sierra Leonean Army (SLA) that had led the military coup
in 1997 were Lrger in number and better organized and equipped than previously believed.
Second, the subsequent stalemate reaffirmed that a strictly military solution to the problem would
be impossible. These two facts hasten the international community and the leadership of the
Economic Community of West African States (ECOWAS) to advocate more forcefully to the
Government of Sierra Leone (GOSL) for a negotiated settlement to the conflict. With the active
support of the ECOWAS Chairman and the United Nations Special Envoy to the Secretary
General, the Government entered into political discussions with representatives of the RUF in
May following a cease-fire agreement. By June a peace agreement had been drafted and the RUF
political leadership had discussed the main elements of the agreement with their field
commanders. On July 7, 1999, the President of Sierra Leone, Ahmad Tejan Kabbah, and the
political leader of the RUF, Foday Sankoy, signed the peace agreement ending the armed conflict
that had plagued Sierra Leone for almost a decade.
1.3   Sierra Leone and its development partners are now in the process of consolidating this
peace agreement, operationalizing a national disarmament, demobilization and reintegration
program, extending humanitarian relief activities and renewing national reconstruction efforts.
The return to Sierra Leone in early October of Foday Sankoy and Johnny Paul Koroma (leader of
the military junta) to take up responsibilities with the current government and to initiate the
political process which will allow the RUF and the AFRC to stand for office in the upcoming
presidential elections is a promising sign that the peace will hold. The international community is
moving quickly to help the govermnent consolidate its current position and to extend financial
assistance to face the challenges of the post-conflict recovery programs.

Background and Summary of the Conflict
1.4   The root causes of the civil war can be traced to the systematic long-term neglect of the
rural areas of the country and the exclusion of certain groups from political decision-making
under previous governments.  Local government was deliberately weakened and lacked
transparency, the judicial system had lost credibility, infiastructure had decayed and the provision
of social services had deteriorated consistently over a period of twenty-five years.
1.5   The immediate causes of the war can be linked to the civil war in neighboring Liberia and
the subsequent establishment and militarization of the Revolutionary United Front of Sierra
Leone. The Government of Sierra Leone supported the ECOMOG peacekeeping operation in
Liberia, which clashed with Charles Taylor's National Patriotic Front of Liberia (NPFL) in 1990.
In retaliation, the NPFL provided military assistance to a small movement of disaffected Sierra
Leonean exiles known as the Revolutionary United Front. The civil war in Sierra Leone began
on 23 March 1991, when the RUF, under the leadership of former Corporal Foday Sankoh,
initiated a guerrilla war in the east of the country. The stated objective of the RUF was to oust
the All People's Congress government of President J.S. Momoh. The Sierra Leone armed forces
were caught by surprise and were ill-equipped to confront the RUF threat. The conflict soon
spread to the southern and central regions of the country.
1.6   The war intensified in late 1994, when the RUF significantly expanded the scale and
scope of its military activities and threatened Freetown. As a result of this escalation of hostilities
and the associated breakdown of civil authority in large areas of the country, an estimated
1.5 million civilians from war-affeted areas fled to towns and cities in search of refuge.
Important mining operations such as Sierra Rutile, the Sieromco bauxite mine and key diamond
fields, were overtaken by the RUF twice in early 1995. This escalation reduced Sierra Leone's
official gross foreign exchange earnings by 500/o, disrupted agricultural production and curtailed
the activities of development agencies and the private sector.
1.7   From mid-1995 onwards, the RUF suffered a series of military defeats as a result of the
introduction by GOSL of Executive Outcomes1 and the gradual strengthening of the camajoh
(civil defense forces (CDF)) movement. With this series of military defeats and following the
multi-party elections in early 1996, the RUF agreed to a cease-fire and entered into direct
negotiations with the new Govermment of Ahmad Tejan Kabbah in March 1996. After several
months of negotiations and further kamajoh military pressure, the discussions culminated in the
signing of the Abidjan Peace Accord on November 30, 1996.
1.8   The Abidjan Accord, however, was not to be fully implemented. While certain elements
of the Accord were put in place - such as the establishment of a bi-partisan Commission for the
Consolidation of Peace - other aspects such as the formulation of a demobilization plan and the
deployment of peacekeeping forces were not. Escalaing hostilities between the armed forces, the
kamajors/CDF, and RUF, and deteriorating security in the provinces, were harbingers of the coup
in May 1997.
1.9   The AFRC coup was different from previous transitions in power in Sierra Leone in
several ways. First, not only was the executive branch overthrown, but the legislative process and
judicial system were also significantly debilitated or, for the most part, destroyed. Second,
nascent recovery efforts of the 14-month old Government were all but suspended with the closing
I  A private security advisory group.



Technical Annex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 6 of 87
of most international donor and development operations, withdrawal of international staff, and
suspension of development aid. Finally, the level of random violence against civilian populations
and both public and private property had significant negative impact on the social fabric of the
countly and on business and investor confidence.
1.10 Restoration of the legitimate Government by ECOMOG in February 1998 brought once
more hope to resolve the conflict. The long process of rebuilding the country could again be
initiated. Important advances were made subsequently, most notably the design of a
comprehensive disarmament, demobilization and reintegration program. While the program
received wide international support and funding commitments, the security situation in the
country deteriorated. This culminated in the brutal January 1999 attack on Freetown. Most
international aid efforts to Sierra Leone were subsequently suspended. It is only with the signing
of the Lom6 Peace Agreement, which incorporates guarantees of free access for humanitarian
agencies to rebel-held areas, that humanitarian relief -, development - and donor organizations are
again looking to expand operations outside the relative safety of Westem Area.
The Lome Peace Agreement
1.11 The Lome Peace Agreement, was signed on July 7, 1999, by President Tejan Kabbah and
Foday Sankoh of the RUF, and witnessed by the Chairman of ECOWAS, the UN Special Envoy
to the Secretary General, and representatives of key regional governments. This Peace Agreement
established the foundation for a lasting peace, return of security and initiation of reconstruction
and development for Sierra Leone. Specifically, the Peace Agreement: (i) reiterates the earlier
signed cease-fire agreement and establishes appropriate bodies to monitor its implementation;
(ii) facilitates the transformation of the RUF into a political party and allows for the RUF and
AFRC personnel to hold public office; (iii) enables the RUF to join a Government of National
Unity through the appointment of Foday Sankoy as chairman of a newly formed Commission for
the Management of Strategic Resources, National Reconstruction and Development, and
providing the RUF with four minister and four deputy minister positions; (iv) establishes a
Commission for the Consolidation of Peace that will oversee the implementation of all structures
and efforts aimed at the promotion of national reconciliation and the consolidation of peace either
in existence or provided for under the Peace Agreement; and (v) establishes a Council of Elders
and Religious Leaders to serve as arbitrators of the Agreement in the event of conflicting
interpretations by the parties. The full text of the Peace Agreement is presented in Attachment 1
to this document.
1.12   While this is not the first agreement of its kind reached between rebel forces and the
GOSL, it is hoped that the level of international support for the accord (and in particular the
participation of the regional governments of Liberia2 and Guinea, as well as Nigeria that supplies
the majority of soldiers to the ECOMOG force) will prove critical in seeing this accord
implemented. The design and preliminary implementation of a comprehensive disarmament,
demobilization and reintegration progrm, which was lacking in previous peace efforts, is also
seen as essential to the long-term success of the accord. In addition, the recent return to Sierra
Leone of both Foday Sankoh and Johnny Paul Koroma to assume their respective responsibilities
as defined in the Peace Agreement is a promising sign that the political will of these two parties is
2 It is noteworthy that President Taylor of Liberia, who it has long been rumored was supporting the RUF
and AFRC against the GOSL, recently brokered the agreement between Foday Sankoy and Johnny Paul
Koroma to return to Sierra Leone as called for in the Peace Agreement



TechnicatAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                   Page 7 of 87
in place. Finally, the active participation of both RUF and AFRC representatives in the process
of operationalizing the disarmament and demobilization activities lends further hope that the
accord will be fully implemented.
Overall Economic and Social Impact of the War and Post-Coup Conflict
1.13   Eight years of war and coup-related unrest have devastated the national economy and led
to large scale social disruption. Substantive damage has been caused to the agriculture and
mining sectors, thereby greatly reducing rural food security and revenue earning capacity of the
country. A large percentage of public infrastructure has been destroyed, or has deteriorated due
to lack of maintenance. The loss of control over resources and diminished government revenues
drastically reduced foreign exchange receipts and fiscal revenues. The civil war has caused a
decline in export earnings from mineral resources, a decline in agricultural productivity and
increased imports of essential food items, such as rice and military equipment.
1.14  In spite of the civil unrest that plagued the country during the 1990's, the Government
had implemented a successful series of stabilization and structural reform policies that had
yielded significant results up until the time of the coup in May, 1997. From 1990/91 to 1996,
annual inflaion was reduced from over 100 percent to around 6 percent, and the budget deficit
was reduced from 12 percent of GDP to 6.8 percent in the period from 1989/90 to 1996. These
gains were achieved while at the same time protecting social sector spending despite budgetary
pressures related to the war. However, nine months of junta rule in 1997, and predominantly
unstable economic and political conditions during 1998 and early 1999 have reversed most of
ese advances. Real GDP was estimated to have contracted by over 20 percent in 1997, and the
overail budget deficit stood at 11.4 percent of GDP in 1998. It is estimated that real output
declined by approximately 9 percent during the first half of 1999. Nonetheless, the economic
siuation during the second half of 1999 is expected to improve with the political stabilization
resulting from the implementation of the July Peace Agreement. Government revenue is
projected to increase, and while budget outlays are also expected to increase in relation to
emergency relief and rehabilitation expenditures, the primary budget deficit for the year as a
whole is projected to be 7.8 percent of GDP. Nonetheless, given the expected resumption of
budgetary and other assistance in the last quarter of the year, the reliance on bank financing to
cover the budget deficit is expected to significantly decline, thereby reducing inflationary
pressure and stemming the fia in gross external reserves. An IMF mission, in October 1999, will
establish with Government the indicative reserve targets for end-December 1999, as part of their
proposed emergency post-conflict assistance policy program for Sierra Leone.
1.15   The social costs of the conflict have been equally high. Delivery of basic social services
has been dramatically curtailed in areas outside of the capital. Since 1991, more thn twenty-five
thousand lives have been lost as a direct result of the conflict. The war and related insecurity
have also caused the internal displacement of an estimated 1.2 million people (out of a total
population of 4.7 million), and most of the rural displaced fled to urban environments
characterized by inadequate housing and poor sanitary conditions. The population of Greater
Freetown alone increased from 731,000 in 1989 to more than 1.5 million by late 1995, thus
further straining already overburdened social services. Many of the displaced have been housed
by extended families, thus also stretching traditional coping mechanisms to their limits. As of
August 1999, the United Nations Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs
(UNOCHA) reports that there are still 150,000 registered internally displaced persons (IDPs).
However OCHA estimates that as many as 1 million people are at risk due to insecurity and the
inability of humanitarian agencies to access large parts of the northern and eastern regions of the



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Counhy: &erra Leone                                                   Page 8 of 87
county. The UN High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) estimates that more than
500,000 people have fled Sierra Leone for neighboring countries since 1991, and that at present
almost the same number remain in neighboring countries.
1.16   The war and subsequent tunmoil have exacerbated already high levels of poverty in Sierra
Leone. Prior to 1991 it is estimated that about 80 percent of the population lived on less than one
dollar per day. Since then, the situation has deteriorated with the widespread destruction of
public infrasucture, massive population displacements, and the withdrawal of foreign
investment. The civil war and post-coup period have also been characterized by economic rent-
seeking. Looting for food, cash crops and other material goods became a means of survival and
often a source of profitable business for elements within both the RUF and the AFRSL.
Moreover, control over the illicit diamond mining and trade in the areas bordering Liberia
became a way for the RUF to finance the procurement of arms and other supplies. These factors
contributed significantly to the prolongation of the war.
1.17   The conflict has also contributed to the weakening of traditional authority structures at
the chiefdom level, and the reduction of trust at the community and family levels, as leaders and
elders have been killed or displaced, women have been sexually abused and children have been
abducted. Systematic attacks on authority figures (both human and symbolic- as in desecration of
secret society "bushes") were a feature of RUF stategy. The remnants of the AFRC junta have
also tried to undermine the existing authority structures in the towns and villages that they have
attacked since being ousted by ECOMOG. Nonetheless, these social structures too have shown
remarkable resilience. Many chiefs were sent out of harm's way by their subjects in order to
protect their authority; the displaced persons camps have typically been organized in accordance
with chiefdom structures; and the command, control and recruitmnent mechanisms for the
kamajoh/CDF movement are closely linked to the chiefdom governance system. Civil society
reaction to and condemnation of the May coup was rapid, the call for a return of the
democratically elected government was loud, and, ironically, the nine-month period of AFRC
rule served to strengthen many local level institutions that were left to fend for themselves in the
absence of any central-level support or control. Since the return of the Kabbah government, civil
society representatives have been both active and vocal in calling for a peaceful settlement of the
9-year conflict.
1.18   The civil war and the coup are clearly both symptoms and causes of the weakness of the
Sierra Leonean state. The long-term decline of state services, a governance system characterized
by corruption, and the politics of regional exclusion were key causes of the civil war. The large
scale destruction of infrastructure, economic resources and social displacement in the course of
the conflicts have further weakened the governance capacity of the state. The recovery and
reintegration process must address the underlying socio-economic causes of the war for Sierra
Leone to resume a path of peaceful and democratic social and economic development.
Security
1.19   Security in Sierra Leone has improved significantly since the low point in January 1999
when AFRC and RUF forces attacked Freetown. A cease-fire agreement signed in May of this
year has held, GOSL and RUF representatives took part in detailed technical discussions in Togo
during May and June, and on July 7, 1999, a Peace Agreement was signed between the GOSL
and the RUF. Since then, RUF and AFRC leadership (Sankoy and Koroma) have retumed to
Freetown and are traveling around the country briefing their respective forces on the peace
agreement, disamament and demobilization; RUF and AFRC representatives have begun



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Counbty: Slerra Leone                                                 Page 9 of 87
participating at operational levels in the implementation of the disarmament and demobilization
efforts; and while some tensions remain, especially in relation to AFRC representation in the
Peace Agreement, these have been defused through negotiations rather than violence. Access to
the capitals of the Southern and Eastern provinces has been restored and while rebel troops still
control large portions of the north and east of the country, they have established procedures and
mechanisms to allow humanitarian agencies access to these areas and the at-risk populations
there. The UN has improved the security rating for Western Area and Southem Province to a
level 3 and the US Embassy has reopened at the end of September, 1999. It is expected that with
the rapid implementation of the Government's comprehensive disarmament, demobilization and
reintegration (DDR) program the security situation in the North and East of the country will
improve rapidly.
Current Situation
1.20   Sierra Leone reached a low point in its nine year rebel war, when RUF and AFRC forces
mounted a well-coordinated attack on Freetown that drove the ECOMOG forces responsible for
maintaining security in the country out of two-hirds of the city. A week-long battle ensued in the
central and eastem parts of the city hat resulted in an estimated 5,000 deaths, as yet uncalculated
destruction of property, and the displacement of more than 50,000 citizens from the eastern end
and outskirts of the city. ECOMOG forces were eventually able to regain control of the city, the
Western Area, and some territory lost to the advancing forces in December and January. A
military stalemate was achieved with the Western and Southern areas of the country controlled by
ECOMOG and CDF personnel, while large portions of the East and North of the country were
held by rebels. The result of this siuation was a clearer realization on the part of the Government
and the international community that had been supporung Tejan Kabbah that a negotiated
settlement with the rebel forces was the only practical option. This led to detailed technical
discussions between the GOSL and RUF leadership, which allowed the RUF leadership
(Foday Sankoh) to meet with RUF field commders. At the same time, a 3-day "National
Consulation on the Peace Process" exercise was undertaken by civil society representatives
aimed at strengtening the Government's mandate in the peace negotiations. Ultimately, in this
context, and with the encouragement and support of the ECOWAS Chairman and the UN Special
Envoy to the Secretary General, the Government and the RUF signed a Peace Agreement in
Lom6 on July 7, 1999.
1.21   The signing of the Peace Agreement has led to almost immediate action on the part of
Government and its major development partners to begin addressing the humanitarian crises that
is looming in large areas of the country (most notably the north and east); the need to disarm,
demobilize and reintegrate a now estimated 45,000 combatants from the forces of the RUF,
AFRC, AFSL and the CDF; and to restart interrupted efforts at rebuilding the lives and
communities that have been adversely affected by the past nine years of conflict. The
Government has helped coordinate the humanitarian efforts of the NGOs, UN agencies and line
ministries, and has revised and updated the National Disarmament, Demobilization and
Reintegration Program based on the changing circumstances post-January 1999. A multi-donor
mission carried out in September 1999 reviewed and reiterated its support of the priorities
previously identified by Government, including: security, demobilization and reintegration of ex-
combatants, implementation of the national reconstruction, rehabilitation and resettlement
program, governance and rule of law, economic management, decentralization and capacity
building. The International Contact Group (ICG) for Sierra Leone is monitoring the
implementation of Government's respective recovery efforts and is seeking to assist the
Govermment to mobilize resources for these priority areas. In terms of security, the Economic



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Titk: Community Reintegration andRehabilitation
Countfy: Sierra Leone                                                Page 10 of 87
Community of West African States has agreed to maintain the presence of the 12,000 person-
strong ECOMOG force to maintain security until a new Sierra Leonean army can be recruited and
trained, and the United Nations has approved a team of 210 military observers (UNOMSIL) to
monitor the disariament and demobilization process and to take receipt of surrendered weapons.
In October 1999, the UN Security Council approved a 6,000 person strong Peace Keeping force
for Sierra Leone.
1.22   The President formally established two government institutions with responsibility for
overseeing the short-term reintegration and rehabilitation efforts of the country: the National
Committee for Disarmament, Demobilization and Reintegration (NCDDR), and the National
Commission for Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation (NCRRR). The overall
managers of each institution (respectively, the Executive Secretary and the Commissioner) have
been appointed and core technical staffed recruited and hired. The Government has also begun
implementing concrete efforts to resume its role as coordinator and facilitator of the overall
recovery and reconstruction efforts of government ministries, NGOs, and community groups.
The NCRRR structure and staffing requirements have been reviewed and revised in careful
consultation with donors, a plan of action and three-year budget have been developed and are
being considered by NCRRR's major partners, and the coordination mechanisms at central and
regional levels have been reestablished and are funcdoning with a high degree of efficiency.
1.23  In 1998, the GOSL also developed a comprehensive program framework for the
disaLmament demobilization and reintegration (DDR) of all ex-combatants (including AFRSL,
RUF, and CDF personnel). The proposed strategies of the DDR Program were discussed at the
special UN conference held in July, 1998, that was well-attended and resulted in renewed
international commitment to Sierra Leone and its programs of national recovery, as well as
support to ECOMOG. The UK Department for Intemational Development (DFID) provided
technical and financial assistance to facilitate the implementation of phase I of the program that
had targeted approximately 7,000 ex-combatants. However, the breakdown in security in late
1998 that culminated in the January 6, 1999, attack on Freetown interrupted activities under the
DDR program, as well as humanitarian and nascent reconstruction efforts in much of the country.
1.24   Subsequently to the January 6t attack on Freetown, the GOSL undertook to review the
status of the DDR Program jointly with key partners including ECOMOG, UNOMSIL, UNICEF,
DFID, EU, World Bank and other civil society stakeholders. This review was initiated in the
context of promising signs that a conducive political environment had developed that would lead
to a political settlement of the conflict. Following the signing of the Lome Peace Agreement, the
program has been restarted with the opening of the demobilization camp in Lungi, continuation of
training activities, and the identification of possible short-term public works employment
opportunities for the ex-combatants. The DDR program is currently moving ahead quickly with
the establishment of necessary reception centers and demobilization camps in different areas of
the country.
1.25   The level of commitment and ownership on the part of the Government for these
initiatives, the substantive progress made in initiating key program activities, and the wide-spread
international donor community support for the Government's efforts encourages hope for the
success of the overall recovery efforts of Sierra Leone and its partners.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 11 of 87
2. GOVERNMENT RESPONSE AND STRATEGY
Government Strategy
2.1   In light of a cease-fire brokered with the RUF in early 1996, the Govermnent of Sierra
Leone carried out an extensive needs assessment of war-affected areas as the basis for
formulating a national plan for the resetdement, rehabilitation, and reconstruction of the country.
The plan, referred to as the National Resettlement, Rehabilitation and Reconstruction Program
(NRRRP), was presented to the donor community at a UNDP Roundtable Conference in
September 1996. At the core of the NRRRP was an initial Quick Action Program (QUAP) that
focused on 17 different sectors or program areas considered crucial for the social and economic
recovery and revitalization of the country. The QUAP specifically targeted short-term priority
areas of post-war reconstruction, focusing on resettlement of displaced persons, demobilization
and reintegration of ex-combatants, re-establishment of basic social services, and reconstruction
of economic infrastructure particularly related to agricultural production. The signing of the first
Peace Agreement between the Government and the RUF in November 1996 gave hope to national
and international efforts to begin the recovery process in earnest. Commitments made in
September began to become project agreements between donor countries, the GOSL, and various
implementing partners on the ground. The Roundtable Conference yielded pledges of
approximately US$230 million, and formal commitments were estimated at 50% of this total by
November, 1996.
2.2    During the same period, the Government elaborated a Poverty Reduction Strategy that
was anchored on the principle of growth with equity, and developed a Good Governance Strategy
that dealt with decentralization of admstration, civil service refonn, strengthening of civil
society, and promotion of transparency and accountability in the public service. These strategy
documents were presented to a Consultative Group of donors in March, 1997, resulting in pledges
of more than US$600 million. The coup of May 1997, however, effectively put the NRRRP, the
QUAP, as well as these other program activities, on hold.
2.3    While President Tejan Kabbah's Government was in exile, and immediately following
the reinstatement of democratic rule in March 1998, the Government considered its options and
the priority actions it would take to ensure rapid recovery of the country upon its return to power.
Two documents were prepared that highlighted the GOSL's vision for national recovery, namely:
Restoration to Democratic Civilian Rule in Sierra Leone; a 90 Day Programme (November
1997), and Post-conflict Recovery Programme; Preparedfor Consultations with Donors - March
1998. The conclusion of the second document is that the original NRRRP and QUAP priorities
and funding esinates remain valid and that these program documents should continue to guide
discussions with the Government's development partners and the donor community in general.
The original and revised GOSL estimates of costs for the QUAP are shown in Table 2-1.
2.4   Innmediately upon returning to power, the Government undertook to develop a
comprehensive program of disarmament, demobilization and reintegration of ex-combatants - a
specific and concerted strategy notably absent from the Government's pre-coup recovery
program.  The Program, described in the National Disarmament, Demobilization and
Reintegration Program (NDDRP) document of July 1998 (amended in April 1999), will target



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitahon
County: Sierra Leone                                                  Page 12 of 87
approximately 45,000 former combatants of the AFSL, AFRC, the RUF, and the CDF in three
phases over a three-year period. The program has been developed with the assistance of several
donors including DFID, UNDP, World Bank, and UNOMSIL, and has benefited from close
Government consultations with ECOMOG. The Government has shown the highest level of
political commitment, mobilized broad support from stakeholders and the international
community, and moved quickly to establish the implementation strategies and structures required
for the program. The NDDRP, estimated to cost approximately US$33.5 million3, was presented
to and received the wide support of the international community at a special conference called by
the UN Secretary General in July, 1998. An initial phase to demobilize 3,000 former anny
personnel held by ECOMOG was initiated in late 1998 and is now being completed after a six-
month hiatus resuling from the attack on Freetown. Additional support for the NDDRP is being
consolidated through the International Consultative Group that was formed as a result of the
July 1998, UN Conference.

2.5   The multi-donor mission to Sierra Leone in May 1998, and the special UN conference in
July 1998, confimed that donor nations and development partners agree with GOSL priorities
3Revised budget estimates for the DDR Program are currently being prepared by GOSL. It is estimated
tha the new budget which considers a larger number of ex-combatants, could climb to approximately
US$50 millionL

Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 13 of 87
highlighted in the national recovery program documents. These same priorities were reiterated
during the most recent multi-donor mission of September 1999 and a number of donors are
currendy updating their commitments and actively financing elements of the NRRRP and
NDDRP. In addition, the Government is currently following up on information requested by
donor agencies and governments during a donors conference held in Washington in
September, 1999.
Program Framework and Resource Requirements
2.6   In view of the diverse and interrelated natre of the problems engendered by the conflict,
as well as the country's long-term socio-economic decline, GOSL recognizes the need for a
comprehensive strategic framework for guiding the reconstruction and recovery processes.
Moreover, since the country is still in transition from war to peace, the program is anchored
within the context of a continuum from relief to development. Within this framework, the short-
run emergency relief, resettlement, rehabilitation, and reintegration measures are intended to lay
the foundation for resumption of normal development activities. To ensure a successful transition
to the development phase, it is recognized that measures would have to be put in place for the
demobilization of ex-combatants and their reinsertion into civil society, as well as for an orderly
rturn and reintegration of the intemally displaced and refugees.
2.7    With the considerable loss of production capacity in most parts of the country and the
deterioration and/or destruction of much of the basic physical and socio-economic infrastructure,
it is essential that quick rehabilitation measures be put in place immediately after the resettlement
of the displaced people. This will contribute to the restoration of an appropriate socio-economic
environment necessary for normal development processes over the medium-term.
Analysis of Commitments to the NRRRP and NDDRP
2.8   Before assessing the commitments, it is important to recapitulate two important
expectations about program financing related to the extent of local participation, and the nature of
-exnal assistance. First, from the outset, it was estimated that the local contribution to the
progran would be in the order of 10-15%. This would comprise contributions by Government,
local communities, the private sector and external sources. Government's contribution would be
in the form of budgetary allocations to cover mainly the local cost of both development and
recurrent expenditures. The contribution of local communities is expected to be principally in
kind, through the provision of local materials and labor for community programs and projects.
Regarding the private sector, the expectation is that it would progressively promote some of the
planned activities, from transportation of relief goods to reconstruction and developmental
activities. Second, with respect to international support, it is expected that funding would come
pardy from re-allocations of already committed funds, the utilization of which had been
constrained by the escalaton of the war. Government recognizes that redirecting existing loans
and grants would have to be negotiated in detail with individual donors and international financial
institutions.
2.9   The most significant commitments to the post-war recovery programs have been made by
the following international organizations: UNDP and other UN agencies, World Bank, European
Union, African Development Bank/Fund, and by the Governments of the UK, US, Germany,
Japan, Saudi Arabia, and others. The discussion below summarizes commitments made by Sierra
Leone's major partners in the recovery program.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Counhy: Serra Leone                                                 Page 14 of87
2.10   The African Development Bank/Fund (AfDBtADF) will provide support to the NRRRP
thrgh its ongoing investment program as well as by providing new resources. With regard to
the latter, the AfDB/ADF has committed approximately US$12.24 million for a new emergency
project that will co-finance, along with IDA, the Government's Emergency Recovery Support
Fund (ERSF). In addition, USS6.2 million from the ongoing Social Action and Poverty
Alleviation (SAPA) Program, together with the newly proposed US$17 million Health project,
will be used to address emergency recovery needs. The Government and ADB have recently
agreed that the implementation anangements for SAPA, a social fund used to support multi-
sector activities including micro-enterprise development and rehabilitation efforts in health and
education, would be streamlined and adapted to the Government's ERSF procedures.
2.11 The European Commission (EC) has initiated implementation of a US$11.45 million
Resettlement and Rehabilitation Program, which was suspended soon after start-up m
February, 1997. The program will be extended to include all 12 rural districts of the country, and
will also target the urban areas adversely affected during and immediately following the junta
regime. The EC will continue its emergency agriculture recovery program, provide an additional
US$2.2 million for seeds and tools to enable farners to resume food production activities, and
will continue supporting refugee assistance for Sierra Leoneans in Guinea and Liberia. Programs
are currently being developed in support of child soldiers and civic education. The EC is also
providing support to the GOSL in the critical area of public financial maagement through
institutional assistance to the Ministry of Finance.
2.12   The Islamic Development Bank (IDB) has committed USS2.75 million for an Emergency
Assistance Project which will finance the provision of medicines, food and other supplies for the
displaced; rehabilitation of peripheral health units and primary schools; and rice seed production.
A Project Coordination Unit has been established within the National Commission for
Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation (NCRRR) to oversee the implementation of the
project. The IDB has appraised its planned assistance and disbursements have commenced.
2.13   UNDP has been and will continue to support the Government's recovery efforts in
several ways. First, US$4.5 million has been committed for the Preventive Development
Program to be implemented through the NCRRR, and approximately US$200,000 has been
allocated in support of local non-governmental organizations and governmental coordination
capacity.
2.14   The Government of Japan committed US$0.6 million for multi-sectoral activities through
the UNDP-managed Trust Fund. The entire amount has been disbursed to the Trust Fund.
2.15   During 1996-97, the United Kingdom provided US$1.6 million for a pilot demobilization
program channeled through the UNDP Trust Fund. A balance of approximately US$0.6 million
is currently being used to support the newly established Executive Secretaiat of the DDR
Program. In addition, DFID has pledged approximately US$10 million in support of the DDR
Program (of which approximately US$4.5 million has been committed since 1998). DFID is also
supporting NGO-implemented emergency agriculture rehabilitation activities, and is also
supporting governance and capacity building for policing and security activities.
2.16 The International Fund for Agriculture Development (IFAD) has discussed with
Government the possibility of reprogramming approximately US$1.5 million from the North
Central Agriculural Project in support of the Government's ERSF to support efforts at
developing a policy framework for community-based sub-project financing, and to assist in the
area of long-term community-level food security.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Counhy: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 15 of87
2.17   The US Government is providing approximately US$34 million for food security,
agriculture recovery, nutrition rehabilitation, child tracing, and reconciliation activities during
1999, and a similar amount is expected for fiscal year 2000. While food aid constitutes
approximately half of this total assistance (US$16.4 million) significant non-food aid is being
provided by several different offices of the US Agency for International Development (USAID).
These inputs are channeled through both NGOs and UN agencies.
2.18   The IDA-financed Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation Project (CRRP) wiln
provide US$25 million to support the social and economic reintegration of IDPs, refugees, ex-
combatants, and the urban unemployed. These funds will be channeled through two principal
implementation mechanisms; the Emergency Recovery Support Fund (ERSF) that will support
social and economic recovery of communities, and the Training and Employment Program that
supports the social and economic reintegration of former combatants. In addition, the Bank-
financing health sector investment project will be restructured to support emergency health sector
rehabilitation needs, and the transport sector project is adequately flexible to allow for support to
recovery related activities as identified and agreed to between the Govermment and the Bank.
2.19   Table 2-2 below summarizes current actual or proposed support to the Government's
national recovery programs. Several donor countries, such as Sweden, Holland, Norway, Canada
and Germany, have indicated their readiness to support the NRRRP and the NDDRP; however,
the specific nature and level of support has yet to be determined.

3. PROGRAM IMPLEMENTATION
3.1   The Government of Sierra Leone has prepared and presented to the international
community two programs for economic and social renewal in response to eight years of armed
conflict and more than two decades of misrule, neglect of social infrastructure, and misuse of
economic assets. These programs are outlined in the National Resettlement, Reconstruction and
Rehabilitation Program (NRRRP) and the Nahtonal Disarmament, Demobilization and
Reintegration Program (NDDRP) documents. Both of these program documents have been
widely distributed to and received support from the international donor community and Sierra
Leone's development partners, first at the UNDP Roundtable Conference held in Geneva in
September, 1996, and more recently at the special UN conference sponsored by the Secretary
General in New York in July 1998.
3.2   Upon being reinstated to power in March, 1998, President Kabbah formally established
the two institutions that would oversee the implementation of the two national programs outlined
in these documents.  These institutions, the National Commission for Reconstruction,
Resettlement and Rehabilitation (NCRRR) and the National Committee on Disarmament,
Demobilization and Reintegration (NCDDR), have limited and time-bound mandates, are staffed
by local consultants, and will operate outside of standard civil service procedures to help maintain
their independence and improve their efficiency. NCRRR will coordinate and monitor
implementation of the NRRRP, while NCDDR will have responsibility for overseeing the
NDDRP.
National Commission for Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation
3.3   In light of the integrated and multi-sectoral needs of reconstruction and recovery, both
Government and key donors have recognized the need for a sectorally integrated mechanism to
oversee the NRRRP. NCRRR has been created to fulfill this role in the same way that the former
Ministry of National Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation was.  Unlike its
predecessor, however, NCRRR does not have a civil service administrative branch. Also, in line
with donor and development partner recommendations, the Commission has rationalized its
structure to better reflect its coordination and monitoring mandate, and has strengthened the focus
on decentralized sub-project appraisal. The organiional structre of NCRRR is shown in
Figure 3-1 on the following page.
3.4    NCRRR will facilitate dialogue between and coordinate the programs and activities of
NGOs, line ministries, donor agencies and other implementing partners active in the emergency
recovery program, and monitor and report on all recovery activities undertaken in the country.
NCRRR will also oversee the implementation of the Emergency Recovery Support Fund (ERSF),
a demand driven community-based social fund designed to facilitate the social and economic
recovery of communities and the restoration of basic social and economic services. The ERSF
targets internally displaced persons (IDPs), refugees, and ex-combatants. However, in keeping
with its inter-sectoral and coordination role, the Commission will not directly implement recovery
activities financed by the ERSF; this responsibility will rest with its partner organizations,
including NGOs, line ministries, public authorities and community based organizations.

The Commission has been established with a finite life span of three years reflecting the short-
term, emergency, focus of the Government's recovery program. The Commission should cease to
operate once the immediate needs of resettlement and emergency rehabilitation are addressed,
leaving the longer-term needs of development to the traditional governmental agencies and NGOs
active in the various sectors.3.5 NCRRR consists of three technical units, one administrative unit,
and three project units, namely:
* Directorate of Programming and Coordination (P&C)
* Directorate of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation (PM&E),
* Information and Sensitization (IS) Unit,
* Administation, Accounts and Logistics Unit (AAL),
* Social Action and Poverty Alleviation (SAPA) Project Unit,
* Islamic Development Bank Project Implementation Unit (EAP) and,
* UNDP Preventive Development Program Unit.
3.6 Each Directorate is composed of a Director and one or more Program Managers
responsible for different functional areas. The IS Unit is headed up by a Program Manager with
one Project Officer as staff, and the AAL Unit is headed by an Executive Secretary and composed
of three additional staff The SAPA and EAP Units are headed by Directors who have between
two and four functional staff, while the UNDP Program Unit is headed by a chief technical
advisor with one national counter-part. Financial management and procurement of all RRR
related sub-projects under the IDA Credit will be handled by a separate Financial Management
and Procrement Unit, which is described below. In addition, four regional offices have been
established to coordinate and oversee NCRRR operations in the three provincial capitals (Makeni,
Bo and Kenema), and in Freetown for Western Area. These regional offices (see Figure 3-2) will
be headed by the Regional Coordinator, staffed with two Projects Officers, a Finance Officer, an
Information/Sensitization Officer, and will be supported by HACU field officers4. The main
responsibilities of the regional office will be:
* information sharing and coordinating activities among NCRRR, line ministries, NGOs and
regional authorities (government and chiefdom);
* providing technical and administrative support to the ERSF Projects Committee in
reviewing project proposals and monitoring project implementation,
* monitoring the work of implementing organizations (line ministries and NGOs) in carrymg
out the regional program; and
* reporting on implementation progress for the national program to the central Commission.
NCRRR is also discussing the possibility of including a United Nations Volunteer for conflict
resolution in each of the regional NCRRR offices.
3.7    District offices will also be established in each of the district headquarters5 and three
staff, namely a District Supervisor and two Community Development Officers, will be placed in
each. This staff will have primary responsibility to facilitate the participation of communities in
the ERSF and to monitor the activities of NCRRR's partner organizations in implementing the
RRR Program.
4   One national staff in all three regional offices and one expatriot staff seconded to the UN Office for
the Coordinator of Humanitran Affairs (UNOCHA) by the Norwegan Refugee Council (proposed
for Makeni and Kenema).
Distict offices in the three provincial capitals will be incorporated into the regional RRR offices,
where space permits, to economize on logistical support requirements.

3.8    All NCRRR technical staff will be hired under one-year renewable performance-based
contracts which are independent of civil service salary and benefits regulations. In addition, all
staff financed by the IDA Credit, UNDP preventive development program, and the AflDB
emergency project will require the approval of the respective donor agency, helping to ensure
that the Commission will be staff with well qualified personnel.

National Committee on Disarmament, Demobilization and Reintegration
3.9   Ihe overall DDR program will be implemented under the guidance and supervision of
the National Committee on Disarmament, Demobilization and Reintegration (NCDDR), a
temporary institution officially established by Government in July 1998. NCDDR will coordinate
a program of assistance to all ex-combatants, which will include the demobilization and
reintegration of AFRC, RUF and CDF personnel, as well as assistance to child and disabled
combatants. NCDDR will also oversee the implementation of the Training and Employment
Program (TEP). The NCDDR is chaired by the President and comprises the following members:
National Security Advisor, ECOMOG Task Force Commander, Deputy Minister of Defense,
Minister of Internal Affairs and Local Administration, NCRRR Commissioner, Minister of
Finance, Minister of Information, Special Envoy of the UN Secretary General, a representative of
the lead donor agency and the Executive Secretary of the NCDDR as its secretary.
3.10   An Executive Secretariat (ES) to the NCDDR has been established to earry out the day-
to-day operational issues related to NCDDR's mandate. The Secretariat relies on local and
international NGOs, Ministry of Education affiliated and independent vocational training
institutions, and private businesses to provide the services required under the TEP. An Executive
Secretary heads up the Secretariat and is responsible for reporting to the GOSL and donors on all
disarmament, demobilization and reintegration support provided under the Credit for ex-



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 20 of 87
combatants. The ES will be responsible for ensuring (i) the planning and implementation of the
individual program components in collaboration with other government departments, NGOs and
donors; (ii) the transparent and accountable administrai  of the program (including procurement
and disbursements); and (ini) monitoring and evaluation. The Govermment has already recruited
technical staff at central level with the support of the PPF and DFID, and the Project will continue
supporting these staff as well as provide for essential goods and services for the Secretariat in
close collaboration with DFID. The ES is composed of four units at national level with
responsibility for: disarmament and demobilization, reinsertion and reintegration, information and
sensitization, and monitoring and evaluation. A military liaison office has also been established
to facilitate cooperation with ECOMOG and UNOMSIL on all security-related aspects of the
program. Financial management and procurement for all DDR activities supported under the
IDA Credit will be handled by a separate Financial Management and Procurement Unit
(described below). The organigram of the NCDDR/Executive Secretariat is shown in figure 3-3.
3.11   Regional Reintegration Offices (RROs) will be established in three regional capitals as
well as in the Western area. Each RRO will comprise 6 staff, namely a regional reintegration
coordinator, two ex-combatant counselors, two employment promoters, and an accountant. The
Govenmnent is currently recruiting RRO staff that would be responsible for:
* information dissemination and counseling;
D administration of reintegration assistance;
D monitoring and evaluation; and
coordination with NCRRR, other government agencies, administrative authorities, local
leaders, and other project interventions that target ex-combatants.
3.12   Each RRO will be assisted by a regional technical committee that will be headed by a
Regional Reintegration Officer and be comprised of representatives from the local administration
and/or sector ministry, taditional or religious leaders; NGOs and local businesses. This regional
reintegration committee will advise the RROs on: (i) community sensitization; (ii) program
implementation; (iii) conflict resolution related to ex-combatant reintegration; (iv) co-ordination
with other rehabilitation/reintegration interventions; and (v) security issues.
3.13 Government recognizes that the activities of NCRRR and NCDDR are not mutually
exclusive and that the overall success of both programs relies on close and integral coordination
between the two institutions. Therefore, the programs have established linkages at the national
policy level and will do the same at the regional implementation level through the various
committees designed to facilitate coordination (described in detail in the next section.)
Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU)
3.14   A Financial Management and Procurement Unit will be established to provide NCRRR
and NCDDR with technical expertise in all aspects related to financial management and
procurement. This unit will be set up and managed by an internationally recruited firm with
project and financial management experience, and will ensure the highest degree of
accountability and transparency in the implementation of the Project and Program. It is expected
that the FMPU will oversee funding provided by several donor agencies for the national RRR and
DDR Programs. The Unit will be headed up by a senior project management expert with
significant international experience, and be composed of three technical units responsible for
accounting and disbursements, procurement, and internal control of all relevant program
resources. The FMPU will work closely with personnel of NCRRR and NCDDR, and with the
Ministry of Finance, in executing all donor-related financial management responsibilities.

4. COORDINATION MECHANISMS IN PLACE
4.1    Overall aid management and coordination in Sierra Leone continues to be weak, although
the Government is making efforts to limit the entry channels for resources and streamline the
control system. The Department of Development (under the Ministry of Finance, Development
and Economic Planning) is charged with the responsibility of coordinating, managing and
monitoring development assistance, of which the NRRRP and NDDRP will be mrajor components
in the near term. However, specific responsibility for the coordination of activities related to the
recovery efforts is the responsibility of NCRRR, while those related to demobilization and
reintegration will fall to NCDDR. Still, donor agencies and international NGOs continue to deal
directly with the respective line ministries, or in some cases regional authorities, with which they
have long-standing relations and a tradition of cooperation. While information on these
respective funding decisions and activities is, for the most part, transmitted to one or more GOSL
office, the Government's capacity to collect and consolidate this information, or to monitor the
work of these partners, is inadequate.
4.2    Nonetheless, Government's recent efforts to better coordinate the emergency relief and
initial recovery phase have been more successful due in large part to the assistance provided by
the Hmanitarian Affairs Coordinating Unit (HACU) of the UN Office for the Coordination of
Humanitarian Affairs (UN-OCHA). Given the complex nature of the situation, and the frequent
need to respond quickly to an emerging crises in a multi-sectoral fashion, GOSL has taken a
number of steps to strengthen coordination. While NCRRR has taken a lead role in this area,
other organizations are involved in the coordination of the National Programs as well. These
organizations and their inter-linkages are described below.
National Level
4.3   Inter-Msrnteial Committee. This Ministerial-level Program Steering Committee was
established to facilitate the smooth operations of all aspects of the NRRR Program. This
Committee is chaired by the Commissioner of NCRRR, and is composed of the Ministers of
Finance, Development and Economic Planning; Agnculture, Forestry and Environment; Local
Govermuent, Land and Housing; Education; Health and Sanitation; and Social Welfire, Gender
and Children's Affairs. The Steering Committee meets regularly to deal with policy issues and to
address problems and constraints to NRRRP implementation, and will provide guidance to
NCRRR.
4.4    National Committee on Disarmament, Demobilization and Reintegration. NCDDR is
the policy making body for the DDR Program chaired by the President and is comprised of the:
National Security Advisor, ECOMOG Task Force Commander, Deputy Minister of Defense,
Minister of Internal Affirs and Local Administration, NCRRR Commissioner, Minister of
Finance, Development and Economic Planning, Minister of Informaution, the Special Envoy of the
UN Secretary General, a representative of the lead donor agency (the British High
Commissioner), and the Executive Secretary of the NCDDR as Committee secretary. NCDDR
formulates national strategies for, and coordinates all governmental activities that support, the
DDR Program; the Executive Secretariat (ES) will be responsible for carrying out the policy and
implementing, on a day-to-day basis, the responsibilities of the Government under the DDR
Program.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 23 of 87
4.5    Consultative Forum. This forum was constituted to provide technical advise to the Inter-
Ministerial Committee on humanitaian relief and emergency recovery activities. The forum is
comprised of representatives from donor agencies, Government line ministries, and non-
governmental organizations, and gathers information from the specialized technical sub-
committees in the areas of agriculture, food aid, water and sanitation, health and nutrition,
education, shelter and roads, child protection, and sensitization and information. This forum
replaces both the inter-sectoral technical committee (composed of representatives of GOSL line
ministnes) and the national inter-agency forum that operated and advised MNRRR pnor to the
coup. The Forum is chaired by NCRRR.
4.6   Spedic Sectoral Commitees. A number of specific technical or sectoral committees are
currently operational and are responding to the needs of information sharing and coordination
among funding agencies, implementing partners and government institutions. Among these are
the national Committee on Food Aid, a Water and Sanitation Committee, an Agricultural Sub-
committee, and the Emergency Health Task Force. These committees discuss and establish
policy guidelines and operational procedures for their respective sectors and inform larger inter-
sectoral bodies (such as the Consultative Forum) of their deliberations. The Project makes the
best use of these bodies and mechanisms to help ensure appropriate levels of coordination of
donor funding and implementing partner activities.
4.7    Commission for the Consolidaion of Peace. This body has been established under the
Lm6 Peace Agreement to ensure the overall implementation of the post-conflict programs
envisioned by Government. The Commission will have as members two representatives of civil
society, and one representative each named by the Government, the RUF and the Parliament.
While the CCP's terms of reference have not yet to be specified, it is assumed that this body will
monitor the activities of the other institutions responsible for the implementation of the recovery
efforts, such as NCRRR and NCDDR.
Regional Level
4.8   Inter-Agency Fora. In response to the emergency relief activities carried out from 1995
to 1997, inter-agency forums (IAF) were formed in each provincial capital to serve as
mechanisms for information exchange and coordination between GOSL line ministries, NGOs,
relevant UN agencies, bilateral donors and community-based organizations. Participation in the
WF is open to all development partners and representatives of beneficiary groups and is chaired
by NCRRR. As with the Consultative Forum at national level, specialized sectoral committees,
such as those dealing with food aid, health and nutrition, or child protection, advise the IAF on
specific technical issues. The I4Fs also serve as the first point of review for subprojects
proposed for financing under the CRRP. Subsequent to the junta, the IAF in Freetown has been
superseded by the Consultative Forum.
4.9   Specd&ic Technical Committees. Numerous sector-specific committees and ad hoc
technical working groups exist at both regional and national level that address issues of technical
norms, procedures and coordination among agencies working in the same sector. Examples of
such committees include the Committee on Food Aid, the Water and Sanitation Committee, the
Demobilization Advisory Group, and the Agriculture Committee. These committees have either
formal or informal links to the regional and national inter-agency committees (i.e., LAF and the
Consultative Forum) and will serve the CRRP in terms of technical review and input to ERSF
sub-project review.



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                  Page 24 of 87
District Level
4.10   Distid Coorinaton Commiees (DCC). Under the NRRRP, DCCs will be formed by
NCRRR distict level staff with the assistance of the local authorities and line ministries, to assist
in the process of assessing local priorities and identifying finding gaps for recovery activities.
The composition of the DCC will include the NCRRR District Supervisor, and representatives of
local NGOs, line ministries, local government and chiefdom authorities.
Donor Coordination
4.11   The UNDP Resident Representative has traditionally assumed responsibility for donor
coordination in Sierra Leone, a country with few bilateral donors and where the dnve for
economic management reforms has been supported by the World Bank/IMF adjustment programs
through the Ministry of Finance. The UNDP Resident Representative is also the UN
Humanitarian Coordinator, which improves the linkage between emergency relief and longer
term rehabilitation assistance. HACUtUN-OCHA assists in coordinating the relief efforts of
NGOs and donors in Sierra Leone and also plays a critical role in providing technical assistance
to NCRRR.
4.12   The Government and the Bank have developed and maintained close and constructive
working relationships with key development partners and stakeholders in the preparation of the
proposed CRRP. Cooperation has been particularly close with DFID and the British High
Commission; the Office of the Special Envoy of the Secretary General and UNOMSIL; the
African Development Bank; the UN fimily, especially OCHA, WFP, UNICEF, UNHCR and
UNDP; the European Union; USAID; and several national and international NGOs. The Bank
has conducted joint missions or overlapped with several key donors including UNDP, DFID,
GTZ, AfDB, UNHCR, IFAD, and USAID's Office of Transitional Initiatives (USAID/OTI), and
has achieved a high degree of consensus on program strategies and respective institutional
support. This effective coordination among the Govermnent's key partners bodes well for
program implementation.

5. OBJECTIVES, RATIONALE AND STRATEGY FOR
BANK ASSISTANCE
Rationale for Bank Involvement
5.1   The GOSL clearly recognizes that demobilization and the subsequent social and
economic reintegration of ex-combatants, IDPs, and refugees are crucial for achieving lasting
peace, stability and economic growth. The Government has explicitly outlined its strategies for
addressing these priorities in two program documents previously noted (i.e., the National
Resettlement, Reconstruction and Rehabilitation Program (NRRRP) and the National
Disarmament, Demobilizanon and Reintegration Program (NDDRP)). The first progran is
based on an extensive needs assessment of war-affected areas carried out by the GOSL in mid-
1996, which was submitted to the international community at a UNDP Round Table Conference
in September 1996 in Geneva. Subsequent to the restoration of democratic rule in March 1998,
the GOSL reiterated the priorities outlined in this first document and the donor community
indicated their agreement with these priorities in the Joint Governent/Donor Communiqu6
issued in May, 1998. The second document, prepared after the return of the Kabbah Government,
was presented to the international community at a special meeting called by the UN Secretary
General in July 1998.
5.2    Both documents have been well received by donor agencies and development partners
active in Sierra Leone, and these organizations have moved quickly to support the program
strtegies outlined therein. Specifically: the United States, the European Union, UN agencies and
non-governmental organizations are assisting with emergency relief food security, and
emergency health services; DFID, UNOMSIL, and the Office of the Special Envoy on the UN
Secretary General are helping to support the demobilization and reintegration of ex-combatants;
and UNDP, the African Development Bank, and the Islamic Development Bank are helping to
strengthen the institutional capacity of the National Commission for Reconstruction, Resettlement
and Rehabilitation.
5.3  The commitment of the GOSL and the international community to the recovery process
presents a unique "window of opportunity" to revitalize Sierra Leone's economic and social
development. This opportunity also brings with it raised expectations as well as conerns over
the Government's ability to nmnage the enormous post-war reconstruction effort facing the
country and to quickly succeed in: (i) consolidating peace and undertaking to disarm and
demobilize approximately 45,000 combatants; (ii) resettling 150,000 IDPs and repatriating nearly
500,000 Sierra Leonean refugees from Guinea and Liberia; (iii) rehabilitating key elements of the
country's rural infrastructure; (iv) providing material, financial and other inputs to Sierra Leonean
farmers to encourage resettlement and the resumption of agricultural production; and (v) restoring
basic social services, particularly primary health care and basic education.
5.4 This challenging post-conflict context provides the backdrop for the Bank's intervention.
Current confidence in, and high expectations of, the Government may be replaced with frustration
and skepticism if order and security are not reestablished, displaced populations are not
reintegrated, and social and economic infiastucture is not rebuilt. Consequently, timely and
adequate donor assistance is critical for longer-term economic development as well as social
stability.



TechnicalAnnex                       Project Titk: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                   Page 26 of 87
Strategy for Proposed Bank Assistance
5.5  IDA is a leading player in Sierra Leone, with projects in two sectors of central concern to
the national recovery efforts- -inauct  and health- and a Project Preparation Facility (PPF)
for an institutional support Project in education. Furthermore, IDA is proposing to support
macro-economic reform in Sierra Leone through an Economic Recovery Credit As such, IDA is
well-positioned to support the Government's multi-sectoral reintegration and reconstruction
efforts. Its involvement is further justified by the amount of resources required for the recovery
programs, the poverty focus of the Project, the need to monitor the macro-economic impact of the
process, and the shortened time frame within which an Emergency Recovery Credit can respond.
5.6 During the immediate post conflict period of two to three years, rapid progress on
reintegration of ex-combatants, economic recovery and reconstruction, in the context of economic
stability wili be essential to the long-term economic development of Sierra Leone. Therefore,
post-conflict transition will be the central theme of IDA's assistance activities over the next few
years. To this end, the proposed CRRP would serve as IDA's primary instrument to support
rehabilitation, reconstruction and reintegration efforts in Sierra Leone, while the Economic
Rehabilitation and Recovery Credit will address short-term balance of payment and budgetary
support through a credit of USS 30.0 million. At the same time, the Integrated Health Sector
Investment Project (Cr. 2827SL) is being restructured, and the Transport Sector Project
(Cr. 2895-SL) is flexible enough to be adapted, to take account of the changing situation and
emergency resource requirements of the country.
5.7 The Government has adopted a phased strategy to the implementation of the national
recovery programs that has been instructed by post-conflict situations in Afiica and throughout
the world. The phases, and the acual and/or proposed financial support of each one, is
summarized in Table 5.1 below. In the short-term, the proposed CRRP will be central to this
phased strategy.

5.8  The first, transition phase, of the Government's strategy incorporates and implements
simultaneously the two major components of the DDR and RRR Programs, and the proposed
Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation Project intends to support these two components.
The first part of this phase will the improve the security situation through the structured
disarmament and demobilization of approximately 45,000 ex-combatants, including members of
the Armed Forces of Sierra Leone (AFSL), RUF and the Civil Defense Forces (CDF). The
second part will address the short-term needs for social and economic reintegration and
rehabilitation of ex-combatants and the vulnerable civilian population. The second development
phase of the Government strategy would affect a progression from short- and medium-term
reconstruction and recovery programs to longer-term development and poverty reduction through
more traditional investment projects.
Project Rationak
5.9   Security, social stability and future economic growth in Sierra Leone will depend, inter
alia, on the successful demobilization and reintegration of ex-combatants, the return of IDPs and
refugees to their communities, and the revitalization of social and economic infrastructure.
Therefore, the Project, designed to provide the needed immediate support for the reintegration of
these groups and to strengthen the social and economic infiastructure in the communities most
affected by the war, will play an important role in the process of continued recovery. It is felt
that the social and economic reintegration of the target group lends itself well to IDA support
because of the Bank's overall poverty focus in its lending program in Sierra Leone and elsewhere.
5.10   Furthermore, one of the underlying causes of the civil war and conflict over the past
eight years has been the long-standing neglect of rural areas by the Central Government, resulting
in a long-term decline of social services and infrastructure and growing disenchantment among
large parts of the population. The Project will assist the process of redressing the urban bias in
national development through a decenralized sub-project identification and implementation
stregy, that should, if successful, rebuild confidence in GOSL's commitment and capacity to
respond to the needs of the society as a whole.
5.11 The underlying Project concept of providing assistance to the internally displaced,
refugees and ex-combatants, as well as to the receiving communities, is based on best practices
and experiences gained in similar IDA member countries and post-conflict situations, and is
deemed appropriate for achieving the development objective of the Project. The targeted public
works program for the urban unemployed, and the training and employment efforts directed at ex-
combatants are also based on successful Bank and other donor agency experiences in numerous
countries. The Project design is based on a collaborative review and assessment of key socio-
economic and other factors that have and will influence the emergency situation and recovery
process. The review was carried out by the World Bank, Government, and major stakeholders in
as thorough a fashion as possible given the changing security situation in-country during the last
three years. A list of all documents and reports from the project preparation phase contained in
the Project file is shown in Attachment 1 to this Technical Annex.
