11. IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
11.1  The implementation structures, responsibilities and specific institutional arrangements for
the Emergency Recovery Support Fund are detailed in the Project's Operational Manual
developed during Project preparation and appraisal, and updated at the time of post-appraisal.
The Operational Manual specifies the roles and responsibilities of both NCRRR and its
implementing partners to access and monitor the use of funds available under the ERSF The
manual, contained in the Project File, also provides the standard forms, guidelines, evaluation
critena and contacts for implementation of the Fund. A similar operational manual for the TEP
has been prepared, is being piloted during the Project's preparation phase, and will be updated
once the Project becomes effective. All other procurement and disbursement procedures and
responsibilities for the institutional support components of the Project will abide by the relevant
standard World Bank and IDA guidelines as specified in the Development Credit Agreement for
the Project.
Implementation Structures
11.2 The GOSL has given the responsibility of overseeing the CRRP to NCRRR and NCDDR.
While these two institutions will coordinate and monitor the CRRP, partner organizations such as
NGOs, line ministry units, parastatal organimzations and community based organizations will be
responsible for implementing specific activities under the Project. The Commissioner, NCRRR,
and the Executive Secretary, NCDDR, will be directly responsible to the World Bank for the use
of Project resources and will jointly report to IDA on the performance of the Project.
11.3 The Project relies on a decentralized implementation structure. NCRRR will have
12 district offices staffed with 3 technicians who will promote community participation and
monitor implementation of ERSF subprojects. It is proposed that, at regional level, NCRRR and
NCDDR will have a joint regional offices (one in each of the provincial capitals; Bo, Kenema and
Makeni, and one in Freetown/Waterloo for Western Area); each staffed with five to six technical
personnel per institution. The regional offices and personnel will coordinate CRRP activities
with regional line ministry offices, NGOs, and other implementing partners; participate in the
approval of ERSF and TEP sub-projects; and report on Project implementation to the national
level offices.
11.4  At national level, NCRRR has three technical units responsible for supporting the CRRP;
the Directorate of Programming and Coordination, the Directorate of Planning, Monitoring and
Evaluation, and the Information and Sensitization Unit. NCDDR has four technical units
responsible for overseeing activities under the CRRP, namely Disarmament and Demobilization,
Reinsertion and Reintegration, Information and Sensitization, and the Monitoring and Evaluation
Units. Each unit is headed up by an experienced and qualified professional who is supported by
one to three technical officers.
11.5  The IDA Credit will help to finance core investment and recurrent costs for both NCRRR
and NCDDR at central and regional levels to facilitate the day-to-day management of the
respective components of the Project. These costs for NCRRR will be shared with UNDP and
AfDB, while costs for NCDDR will be shared with DFID and other interested donors.



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Titk: Commnunity Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 52 of 87
Due to weaknesses in local, in-country, capacities resulting from the coup and prolonged civil
service decline, and taking into consideration the temporary nature of both the NCRRR and
NCDDR, Government will delegate responsibility for the Project's financial management and
procurement functions to a separate and autonomous unit to be contracted through international
competitive bidding procedures. This unit, to be known as the Financial Management and
Procurement Unit (FMPU), will manage all procurement for NCRRR and the Executive
Secretariat of NCDDR, be responsible for all contracting and disbursements under the Project,
oversee management of the local Multi-Donor Trust Fund account, and ensure proper record
keeping and reporting for the Credit in accordance with appropriate World Bank guidelines.
Project Coordination
11.6  Project Coordination will be achieved through several existing mechanisms operational at
national and regional levels. First, there are general coordinating institutions, such as the
Consultative Forum at national level, and the Inter-agency Forums at regional levels. Both of
these structures, which are described in more detail in Chapter 4, serve a particularly important
role in the CRRP. The Consultative Forum helps to coordinate activities across sectoral lines and
between NGOs and line ministries with NCRRR. This is particularly relevant in the emergency
recovery activities where rapid technical responses call for integrated information and
public/private cooperation. This Forum meets fortnightly and is chaired by NCRRR. Regional
Inter-agency Forums (IAF) serve to keep the CRRP in close contact with the key implementing
partners at regional level, and to provide a forum to discuss and resolve local level
implementation concerns or to respond to emergencies. The IAFs will also help in the initial
review of ERSF sub-project proposals to ensure appropriate targeting and avoid overlap or
conflict with other implementing partners. The LAF also meets fortnightly and is chaired by the
Regional Coordinator of NCRRR.
11.7  Secondly, there are technically specialized inter-agency bodies that will contribute to
improving Project coordination and implementation. These bodies, such as the Committee on
Food Aid, the Child Protection Committee, and the Health and Nutrition Committee, exist at both
national and regional levels, are composed of the relevant line ministry and NGOs active in the
given technical area, and liaise with the general coordination bodies at the different levels. These
institutions will serve the Project in two ways; by establishing and enforcing technical norms or
standards in the given technical area concerned, and, possibly, as sub-project evaluators.
11.8  In-country, the exceptionally high and constructive level of coordination exhibited in
Sierra Leone is the result of a strong working relationship that the UN Office for the Coordination
of Humanitarian Affairs has established with the Government and the NGO community. It is
expected that UN/OCHA will continue to play a critical role in supporting and facilitating this
type of coordination to the benefit the Project.
11.9   On the international level, the Government has taken important steps to improve
coordination with its development partners. For instance, the International Contact Group (ICG)
was established following the UN special meeting on Sierra Leone in July 1998. Chaired by
DPID, the ICG strives to assist the Government to consolidate support for the DDR phase of its
national program. In addition, the Government has requested that the World Bank establish a
Multi-Donor Trust Fund that will be used to facilitate financing of DDR-relatd activities by
interested donor countries and organizations. This formal consultative body and administrative
mechanism will greatly enhance the level of coordination between Government and



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 53 of 87
its development partners that, in turn, will help to ensure more efficient use of resources during
project and program implementation.
Project Monitoring
11.10 Performance monitoring of the CRRP will focus on two distinct levels: first, at overall
program level for the NRRRP and the NDDRP (i.e., the development objectives, outcomes and
core assumptions and preconditions as shown in the Project's Summary Table contained in
Attachment 3), and second, at detailed Project component and activity level (outputs,
implementation, managerial efficiency, and detailed progran assumptions- again indicated in the
Project Summary Table). This table is a Logical Framework matrix that includes a detailed list of
performance indicators for program objectives and outcomes, Project outputs and inputs, and core
assumptions and related risks. These indicators will be used to measure the performance of the
NDDRP and NRRRP, as well as the CRRP. Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) Systems will be
established at the NCRRR and the Executive Secretariat of the NCDDR The two systems will be
developed using the same software platform, and be compatible in terms of coding of basic data.
Thus it will be possible to follow ex-combatants from the targeted program (i.e., the NDDRP) to
the broad-based program (i.e., NRRRP).
i 1.11  The M&E system of NCRRR will be designed to allow the Commission to (i) analyze the
effectiveness of the ERSF review process; (ii) quantify sub-project outputs by sector, by region,
and by beneficiary type - including ex-combatants - and gender; (iii) assess level and quality of
community and local institutional participation in sub-project design and implementation; and
(iv) measure the impact of assistance in terms of increased agricultural production, expansion of
social services, increased economic activity, and strengthening of community institutions. The
M&E system of the NCDDR will enable the Executive Secretariat to, among others: (i) establish
detailed, gender desegregated, socioeconomic baselines/profiles; (ii) trace overtime participation
of individual ex-combatants in the DDRP (reception of assistance package, participation
orientation, taining and reintegration activities); (iii) assess implementation efficiency;
(iv) assess the level of social and economic reintegation of ex-combatants.
11.12 The Project will monitor the institutional strengthening of NCRRR and NCDDR in
several ways. First, Government will report on the delivery of all identified Project-financed
inputs as part of the quarterly statements of accounts and progress reports. Government will also
review and analyze progress made in implementing work plans (shown in Attachment 8) as part
of the quarterly progress reports. The Project will also monitor how quickly key Project staff
have been hired, and the respective regional offices operationalized. Timely submission of all
Project related reports (quarterly statements of accounts, semi-annual progress reports, and semi-
annual audit reports) will also serve as a measure of institutional capacity. The successful
operation of the FMPU will be judged based on the quarterly consultancy reports to be provided
by the contacted firm; the timeliness and accuracy of SOE's, payment requests, and other
applications and withdrawals; the quality of procurement documentation submitted for IDA non-
objection; and the efficiency of the procurement process. Finally, general Project implementation
will be judged based on the semi-annual audits reports and the qualified observations, if any,
therein.
11.13 The successful establishment and operation of both the ERSF and the TEP will be also be
closely monitored. Both are innovative implementation stategies and based on several critical
assumptions that need.to be verified. For instance, the degree to which inter-agency bodies and
technical commitees will effectively serve in sub-project review, the level of community



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 54 of 87
involvement in sub-project design, and local NGO capacity for sub-project implementation are
areas where certain assumptions need to be tested. These issues will be analyzed in detail as part
of a comprehensive review planned for the Project following the six-month pilot phase. The
Govemment will also report to the Bank on other sub-project implementation indicators such as
the amount of time it takes from proposal submission to Projects Committee review to contracting
(in the case of successful proposals); level of committed finds; number and gender of direct sub-
project beneficiaries; numbers and value of sub-projects by sectoral and geographic area, and type
of implementing partner to determine if any biases exist in sub-project selection; and average lag-
time for authorized sub-project disbursements.
11.14 Beyond the Project's institutional structures and implementation mechanisms, the
Government will also tack and report on the output and impact indicators specific to the sub-
projects being financed under the Project. For instance, basic data on agricultural rehabilitation
and production, refurbishment of infiastructure and delivery of basic services, reconstruction of
economic assets and revival of productive activities, and rebuilding of social institutions will be
collected through the Project's monitoring system. These data will, over time, indicate to both the
Government and the Bank if the Project is achieving its general objectives.
11.15 Each implementing agency (NGO, line ministry, etc.) will be responsible for providing
the regional NCRRR and NCDDR offices with information on monthly sub-project outputs in
either computerized or written form. This data will be consolidated at the regional level,
reviewed by the regional ERSF Comnittee and respective institution, and sent forward to the
central level monitoring units of either NCRRR or NCDDR for analysis and reporting to funding
agencies. The form and substance of this sub-project monitoring infornation will be defined as
part of the formal contract entered into between the Government and the implementing partner.
11.16  The Directorate of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation of NCRRR, and the Monitoring
and Evaluation Unit of NCDDR have overall responsibility for monitoring and reporting to the
Bank on the implementation of the CRRP. These units will be responsible for developing the
quarterly progress reports that will contain consolidated information on the Project's institutional
and physical progress. The FMPU is responsible for preparing the quarterly statements of
accounts and providing relevant financial information to the Commission and the Executive
Secretariat for the semi-annual progress reports.
11.17 Both M&E units will set up and maintain a monitoring and evaluation system that will be
compatible and allow for the integration of data between both institutions. Central-level Program
Managers from each institution will be responsible for ensuring that regional offices submit
monthly consolidated progress reports, maintaining and updating the M&E database, analyzing
performance data, and helping in the design and implementation of specialized studies as needed.
A database program will be designed for the collection and analysis of Project monitoring data
using off-the-shelf computer software. Consultants have assisted NCDDR in the design,
computenzation, set-up and initial data entry for the Lungi registration activity, and technical
assistance is currently being provided through the Multi-donor Trust Fund to design and develop
the overall DDR M&E system. Similar assistance will be provided for the development of
NCRRR's M&E system using PPF funds or through a grant from DFID. The TORs for this
position are shown in Attachment 10). On-going technical assistance support is also envisioned
under the Credit to help in the analysis of data, training of staff in system use, and the
development and conduct of specialized beneficiary assessments. Each Project year, the
Government and the Bank will agree on a list of indicators, and annmal target levels, to be used to



TechnicalAnnex                                       Project Title: Community Reintegration andRehabilitation
Country: &erra Leone                                                                                    Page 55 of 87
monitor the performance of the Project. This list will be drawn from the indicators shown in the
Project Summary Table in Attachment 3.

11.19 ERSFSub-projects. The Emergency Recovery Support Fund is a social fund delivery
mechanism designed to support demand driven community based sub-projects prepared jointly by
communities  and  selected  implementing  partners.    Therefore,  specialized  procedures  for
contracting with implementing agencies (NGOs, line ministries, etc.) have been designed to take
into consideration the substantive involvement of both the community and the implementing
organization in the preparation and implementation of the sub-project. These procedures are
described in detail in the Project's Operational Manual. The sub-projects will be carried out by
implementing partners with technical oversight from NCRRR, NCDDR and relevant line
ministries as appropriate.



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitaion
Counimy: Sierra Leone                                                Page 56 of 87
11.20 Sub-projects will be prepared in conformance with broad sectoral guidelines established
for the CRRP. Review and evaluation of both sub-projects and potential implementing partners
are based on detailed criteria that measure institutional capacity, project coherence, financial
soundness, community participation, environmental impact and sub-project sustainability.
Appropnate technical staff from NCRRR, NCDDR, and line ministries will review both
organizaions and sub-project designs using standard criteria, technical specifications and unit
costs by sub-project type.
11.21 NCRRR and NCDDR will have responsibility for overseeing the contracting of
implementing agencies for sub-projects. The Finanial Management and Procurement Unit will
ensure that contracting is done in accordance with appropriate World Bank Guidelines. Regional
inter-agency committees will have authority to approve sub-projects up to US$50,000 in value;
projects above this threshold will require cental-level NCRRR1NCDDR approval. World Bank
prior review will be required for sub-project contracts valued at USS200,000, or more. The
capacity of the regional committees, NCRRR, NCDDR and the FMPU to adequately manage the
procurement responsibilities under the Project will be tested during a pilot phase carried out with
PPF support.
11.22 Goods. Procurement of goods (including vehicles, equipment, and furniture) under the
institutional support sub-components of the Project will be carried out in accordance with World
Bank procedures as laid out in, "Guidelines, Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits" of
January 1995 (revised August 1996 and September 1997). Contracts for goods estimated to cost
US$200,000 or more will follow standard international competitive bidding procedures
acceptable to the World Bank. Contracts for less than US$200,000 (up to an aggregate amount of
USS200,000) may be procured using national competitive bidding procedures per paragraphs 3.3
and 3.4 of said Guidelines. Contracts for goods estimated to cost less than US$50,000 may be
procured using national shopping (up to an aggregate amount of US$150,000) and international
shopping (up to an aggregate amount of US$100,000). Prior World Bank review for bidding
documents and contract award will be required for any single contract in excess of US$100,000.
11.23 The Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU) will have responsibility for
compiling procurement requests and preparing standard documentation to effect all procurement
under the Project. World Bank standard bidding forms, documents and contracts will be used by
the Unit for this purpose. The FMPU will also assist sub-project implementing agencies prepare
standard bid documents and carry out bulk procurement of necessary inputs as and when
required.
11.24 Serices. All technical assistance and consulting services under the Project will be
procured in accordance with the standard guidelines for the selection and employment of
consultants by World Bank borrowers (January 1997, revised September 1997 and January 1999).
All technical assistance services worth US$50,000 and more will be contracted for using Quality-
and Cost-Based Selection procedures as described in Section 11 of the Guidelines. Audit services
required under the Project will be secured using least-cost procedures in accordance with
pargraph 3.6 of the Guidelines, and all local technical assistance needs valued at less than
US$50,000 per contract will be selected based on consultants' qualifications as described in
pargraph 3.7 of the Guidelines.
11.25 Service contracts estimated to be worth more than US$50,000 for individuals, and
US$100,000 for finns, will require World Bank prior review and no-objection for: (i) the terms of
reference for the services, (ii) short-list of individuals or firms, (iii) request for proposal,



TechnicalAnnex                          Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                          Page 57 of87
(iv) techmical evaluation, (v) final evaluation report and recommendation for selection, and
(vi) draft contract. Furthermore, World Bank prior review and non-objection will always be
required for: (i) tenms of reference for services to be financed under the Credit, (ii) relevant
documentation for services to be contacted for using single source method of procurement, and
(iii) the modification of any service contract that exceeds, or would exceed as a result of the
modification, the previously indicated prior review thresholds. The Financial Management and
Procurement Unit will ensure that appropriate standard documentation (including RFPs,
evaluation reports, lump sum or complex time-based contrats, etc.) are prepared and that World
Bank guidelines are applied in contacting technical assistance services for the Project.
Procurement Planning and Monitoring
11.26 Prior to Project start-up, the Government will prepare the procurement plan for the
overall Project, and provide a draft General Procurement Notice for World Bank non-objection,
and subsequent publication in United Nations Development Business. Subsequently, the FMPU
will take principal responsibility for coordinating all aspects of procurement under the Project for
NCRRR and NCDDR.
11.27 The World Bank will monitor the proper implementation of procurement procedures
continuously for those goods and services which exceed the prior review thresholds as described
above. For other contracts that fill below these thresholds, the World Bank will monitor the
approprate application of Bank procedures through post reviews during the semi-annual
supervision missions. Furthermore, the Bank will require that the audit firm carrying out the
semi-annual audits for the Project assess and provide an opinion on whether or not proper
procurement procedures are being followed as defined in the Credit Agreement.

11.30 The Government will open an account in a commercial bank acceptable to IDA for the

for all Government counterpart contributions to the Project. The FMPU will manage both
accounts on behalf of the Government; two signatories will be required on both accounts,
one from the FMPU (the Unit Manager or his/her designee) and one from NCRRR (either the
Commissioner or the Deputy Commissioner) for related NCRRR expenditures or commitments,
or one from NCDDR (either the Executive Secretary or the Director of Reinsertion and
Reintegration) for related expenditures or commitments. Replenishment of the Special Account
will be made based on satisfactory review of appropriate documentation prepared and submitted
by the Government. The Government will anange for replenishment of the Project Account on a
quarterly, or as need, basis. Withdrawal applications for direct payment and special commitments
wil be made for payments of no less than 20% of the Special Account value. All necessary
documentation supporting Project related disbursements will be maintained by the FMPU for
inspection by Bank supervision missions and independent auditors.

itself with in-kind contributions. The table below indicates the level of financing for each

Transparency and Accountability
11.35 The Government fully appreciates the need for transparency and accountability in the
Project implementation process to avoid duplication of effort and unnecessary wastage of scarce
resources. Inter-Agency Forums (IAF) and Projects Committees at regional and national levels
will provide an open environment for the interaction of Government, implementing partners, and
target beneficiaries in relation to activities financed under the Project. These fora will serve in
the selection of implementing partners and sub-project activities based on clear and objective
criteria. The sub-project selection criteria will be widely publicized and disseminated among all
potential implementing agencies; NCRRR has already taken the initiative to provide this
information to the partner organizations. Furthennore, the sub-project review process will be
recorded and subject to external audit if questions anse. Finally, the inter-agency nature of the
IAF and the ERSF Projects Committees provides additional transparency to the respective review
processes.
11.36 Once sub-projects are approved for implementation, funds will be made available to
implementing agencies as stipulated in the corresponding contracts. Systems will be developed
and staff contracted that have direct and ongoing responsibility for overseeing the use of physical,
miaterial and financial resources, and in measuring performance of the Project based on agreed
upon input, output and outcome indicators. Monthly technical and financial reports, and quarterly
progress reports will be prepared by implementing partners and reviewed by NCRRR and
NCDDR staff and the Projects Committees. An evaluation report will be prepared for all sub-
projects at the end of the program period. NCRRR and NCDDR will also compile monthly
physical progress and quarterly qualitative progress reports on Project implementation as well as
conduct periodic site surveys and beneficiary assessments of sub-projects for IDA and other
donor review.
11.37 Ultimately, the proper application of Project criteria and guidelines will be ensured
through semi-annual audits of Project accounts by an independent public accounting firm. In
addition to providing an opinion as to the status and use of Project funds, the auditor will also
assess Government and sub-project implementing partners' compliance with the appropriate
procurement and disbursement requirements under the Project. IDA will also carry out at least
semi-annual supervision missions to help maintain the highest degree of accountability.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 61 of 87
12. SPECIFIC DELIVERY MECHANISMS
12.1  The unique naure of the Government's programs to address both reinsertion and
rehabilitation efforts call for specialized implementation structures and delivery mechansms. In
response, the Government has prepared detailed procedures and guidelines for the Emergency
Recovery Support Fund (ERSF) and for the Training and Employment Program.. The Operations
Manual for the ERSF was developed in 1997 and was updated in late 1998. Similarly,
procedures for the TEP were developed in 1998 and have been updated and refined in 1999. Both
Operations Manuals have been reviewed by IDA and the most recent versions of these manuals
are contained in the Project File. While aspects of both the ERSF and the TEP have been
highlighted in the previous sections of the Technical Annex, this chapter more fully describes the
systems and procedures central to the operation of these two program funding mechanisms.
The ERSF
12.2  The Emergency Recovery Support Fund is a demand-driven social fund that allows the
Government great flexibility in responding to the needs of recovery and reintegration. The Fund
will be used to address the resettlement and reconstruction efforts as well as support labor-
intensive public works net program that are of concern to the NCRRR.
12.3  The ERSF was developed by the Government in recognition of three important facts
about post-conflict emergencies in general and the state of Sierra Leone in particular: (i) it is
essential that assistance be delivered to the people and communities most affected by the conflict
as quicldy as possible so that further deterioration can be avoided, (ii) a multi-sectoral approach is
required given the extent of social and economic destruction associated with conflicts, and
(iii) the GOSL line ministries and other authorities could not be counted on to respond as required
given their bureaucratic and overly centralist natures, inadequate inter-ninisterial coordination,
and their overall weakened capacities. The decentralized and autonomous nature of the Fund, and
the fact that it builds on the implementation capacity of local and intemational NGOs, as well as
local level government departnents, is an appropriate design and one that responds well to the
challenges of post-conflict situations. The ERSF is being piloted under the PPF, and the
Govemment intends to conduct a detailed review of its operation after six months to make
appropriate changes to the implementation strategies or administrative systems. Terms of
reference for the assessment of the pilot phase of the Project and implementation of the ERSF are
contained in Attachment 12.
12.4  Implementation of the ERSF depends on a decentalized management structure and inter-
agency collaboration. Six structures are critical to the Fund: (i) the existing regional Inter-agency
Forums, (ii) the regional ERSF Projects Committees, (iii) the technical secretariat based in each
Regional NCRRR office, (iv) the existing technical committees or task forces also operating in
each of the three regional (provincial) capitals, (v) the National ERSF Projects Committee, and
(vi) the Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU). Each body and its general
responsibilities are described below.



TechnicalAnnex                         Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                         Page 62 of87
12.5   The existing Inter-agency Forums will provide broad-based, multi-sectoral and inter-
agency feedback to the sub-project approval and monitoring process. This body is chaired by the
NCRRR Regional Coordinator and serves as the forum for general information exchange and
identification of potential problems or conflicts in ERSF project design and implementation. All
proposed projects will be presented to this body before a detailed review is conducted by NCRRR
staff and the appropriate technical body so as to draw upon the collective knowledge of the
broader development community in the regions. Membership in this forum is open to all
interested parties and potential implementing partners throughout the region.
12.6 Each region will establish an ERSF Projects Committee which will have responsibility
for reviewing and approving sub-projects up to the threshold level of $US 50,000, equivalent.
This Committee will be composed of 2 NCRRR staff (the Regional Coordinator and one other
staff member"1) the HACU regional officer, the regional MOFDEP representative, and one
representative of the implementing partners who will be nominated by the Inter-agency Forum.12
The Regional Coordinator will chair the Committee.'3
12.7   Each region will have an ERSF technical secretariat, consisting of two projects officers
and one finance officer, who will carry out the day-to-day adminmtrave tasks associated with
ERSF sub-project review and monitoring. While the secretariat is responsible to the Projects
Committee, on a day-to-day basis these staff will report to the regional NCRRR Coordinator.
12.8   As neither the ERSF Projects Committee nor the technical secretariat will necessarily
have all the information or technical knowledge to adequately assess all types of proposals,
existing technical sub-committees will be called upon to review specific technical aspects of
sub-project proposals submitted to the ERSF. The NCRRR Regional Coordinator would seek the
cooperation of the relevant technical committees or ad hoc groups through the appropriate line
ministry or public authority (such as SLRA), and will have access to necessary resources to
facilitate individual and committee members input to this process.
12.9   A National ERSF Projects Committee will be formed at central level to: (i) approve or
reject all recommended sub-projects that exceed the regional approval threshold of US$50,000'4,
(ii) review and approve all urban public works projects that target the unemployed of Greater
Freetown, and (iii) review and approve all project proposals that are national in scope such as
those presented by central level line ministries. The National Committee will be chaired by the
NCRRR Director for Programming & Coordination, and wil include the Director for Planning,
Monitoring and Evaluation; the Director of the SAPA Program; a representative for the
10 In order to ensure smooth processing and a period for regional capacity-building, all sub-projects above USS25,000

the donors that support the ERSF.
12.10 Finally, the Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU) will be
established to assist NCRRR with all aspects of contracting, disbursements, financial
management and reporting in relation to the ERSF, as well as other donor financed activities
within the Commission. Once an ERSF project is approved and contracts are signed by the
Commission, the FMPU will manage all direct payments made to implementing partners and will
ensure that financial reporting and record keeping is in line with GOSL and donor requirements,
and internationally accepted accounting standards.

ERSF Sub-Project Proposal, Review & Approval Procedures
Sub-Project Proposal Development
12.11 Potential implementing partners first contact NCRRR to receive appropriate guidance in
preparing a sub-project proposals. For this purpose, NCRRR has developed guidelines and
standard formats to assist implementing partners in the proposal development process. While
information will be available at all NCRRR central, regional, and district offices, partners will be
encouraged to contact the appropriate regional or district level office nearest the point where the
sub-project would be implemented.
12.12 Implementing agencies will present project ideas, or concepts, to the Inter-agency Forum
for review and comment prior to submission of a full proposal. This review is designed to ensure
better coordination of development activities in the region, and to identify any potential problems
or concerns in the design of the project. The implementing partner will be encouraged to present
the sub-project concept to the IAF directly, however, if an organization cannot be present at the
IAF meeting, NCRRR staff (either district or regional) can present the sub-project concept on
behalf bf the implementing partner. NCRRR will develop a standard form that implementing
partners can use to prepare and submit this brief project summary to the ERSF project officer at
the regional NCRRR office. The project officer would then determine when the summary would
be presented to the LAF based on the volume and backlog of proposals, and the other duties of the
LAW. A project concept must be reviewed by the L4F before it can be consideredforjunding by
NCRRR
12.13 Once the LAF has reviewed and commented upon a project idea, an implementing partner
can present a full sub-project proposal to NCRRR taking into consideration any comments or
concems raised by the LAF. Sub-project proposals should be submitted directly to the NCRRR
regional office, however, district and chiefdom based organizations may submit proposals to
NCRRR through the district offices if more convenient. District staff will have no direct
responsibility for reviewing proposals, however, and will transmit all proposals (and project
concepts) directly to the regional office for consideration. To be eligible to have a proposal
considered under the ERSF, an NGO or CBO must have submitted an "NGO Profile" form to
NCRRR.lS
ProposalReview
12.14 Project proposal review will be the main responsibility of the ERSF secretariat staff (the
Projects and Finance officers at the regional NCRRR office). These staff will first ensure that a
sub-project and the applicant organization meet basic eligibility criteria (as described in the
Project Operational Manual). At this same time, the ERSF secretariat staff will verify that any
comments or concerns raised by the IAF have been taken into consideration in the sub-project
proposal. If a sub-project or organization is found to be ineligible based on the basic criteria of
the ERSF, or if the issues raised by the LAF have not been addressed or clarified, the proposal will
15 The NGO profile can be suibmtted simultaneously with the first sub-project proposal.

be retumned to the proposing organization with a letter explainig why the sub-project could not
be considered for funding.

technical review of sub-projects. A technical sub-committee will be required to review and
respond to the specific technical issues within 5 days of receipt of a proposal (or specific
technical questions) from NCRRR. The sub-committee will review sub-project proposals to
determine their technical viability, propose   modifications (if any) and indicate their
recommendation of the sub-project on technical grounds. Member or members of the sub-
committee will be able to visit the site of a sub-project or the offices of an applicant agency in
reviewing the proposal.  The Environmental Protection Department of the Ministry of
Agriculture, Forestry and Environment (MAFE) will assist NCRRR with any sub-projects for
which environmental concerns require further review and analysis. NCRRR regional offices will
make appropriate resources (e.g. supplies, logistics, trekking allowances, etc.) available to the
technical sub-committee members to ensure adequate and timely action (including MAFE
environmental assessments) is taken.
Sub-Projed Approval
12.18 Recommendations from both the ERSF secretariat and respective sub-committee will be
consolidated by the Project Officers and passed on to the ERSF Projects Committee. This
Committee will meet fortnightly to review the technical recommendations and decide to either
approve or reject a sub-project proposal, up to a threshold of US$50,000, equivalent. For sub-
projects above this threshold, the regional ERSF Projects Committee may either reject or
L~rcdn~fl4 a proposal to the central level for approval. For this purpose, a National ERSF
Projects Committee will be formed and be given the authority to approve projects over
US$50,000. The national committee will be composed of the NCRRR Director of Programming
& Coordination (chair); the Director of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation; the Director of the
SAPA Progam; one representative from the Development Department of MOFDEP; and one
representative from UN/OCHA. The sub-project review and approval process is illustrated in
Figure 12-3.
12.19 NCRRR will manage different sources of funding from different donor agencies.
Nonetheless, it is hoped that the procedures presented here can be applied to the greatest extent
possible to all outside funding. However, early in the approval process, the Commission will
determine which funding mechanism at its disposal would be the most appropriate for a given
project. By so doing, NCRRR can then take appropriate intenal administtive action to seek
any special approvals for different programs (such as SAPA) or funding agencies to reduce the
need for different external procedures or requirements of potential implementing partners.
ERSF Implementation Arrangements
Contrading
12.20 Once a project has been approved, the ERSF Projects Officer in each region will prepare
the contract for signature based on a standard contract fonnat and required attachments. The
contract will include a summary description of the project, a detailed implementation plan,
project budget and disbursement schedule, and customized forms for physical and financial
progress reporting. Contracts for sub-projects valued at less than US$50,000 will be signed by
the Regional Coordinator/NCRRR, contacts in excess of this threshold will be signed by the
Commissioner of NCRRR.

Disbursements and Financial Control
12.21 The Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FWMPU) will disburse funds to the
implementing partners for project activities once the Commission has authorized the contract. All
disbursements will be made based on monthly financial reports, adequate documentation of
claimed expenditures, and periodic requests for payment submitted by implementing partners to
the regional ERSF Finance Officers. Inplementing partners will submit monthly reports and
associated documentation to the Finance Officers no latter than 10 days after the close of the
preceding month. These reports and requests for payment will be reviewed and consolidated at
regional level and sent to the FMPU for final review and payment processing. The same
consolidated report is sent to the ERSF Projects Committee for monitoring purpose. In the event
of any questions or concerns raised by the regional Finance Officers, the Projects Committee,
and/or the FMPU, the financial officer will follow-up directly with the implementing partners.

12.22 All financial reporting and disbursement requests will be carried out per the terms of
conract between the Government and the implementing partners, and all forms for such
transactions will be provided to the implementing partners as attachments to the standard
contract. Unless issues or questions arise regarding reported expenditures and the status of

withheld from reimbursement

12.23 NCRRR will require that all sub-projects valued at more thal $50,000, equivalent be
audited by an independent public accounting firm. The audit should include a review of the
contactual obligations and restictions for the ERSF sub-project. If an implementing partner
already conducts annual external audits of its accounts, then this audit will be adequate for
NCRRR and ERSF purposes, provided that it also reviews the contractual obligations under the
ERSF. The incremental costs for such audit will be an allowable expense under the ERSF sub-
project activity.  If a sub-project is valued at less than $50,000, equivalent, and if the
implementing partner does not have an independent audit of their accounts conducted annually,
NCRRR will require that all original vouchers for all expenses claimed under ERSF be submitted
to NCRRR as part of the monthly financial report. Otherwise, copies of invoices and vouchers
will be adequate for the purposes of financial reporting.
12.24 To help fwrther guarantee Project accountability, the Internal Control Unit of the FMPU
will be authorized to carry out spot checks of ERSF sub-project expenditures reported by
implementing partners. Finally, the semi-annual audits of the overall NCRRR and NCDDR
operations will also include a random testing of ERSF sub-project activities and expenditures.
Procurement Procedures for Sub-projects
12.25 Prior to entering into contract with any implementing partner for an ERSF sub-project
that would involve substantial procurement, the Government should be satisfied that the systems
and procedures of the responsible institution will ensure transparent and cost-effective
procurement. All such sub-project procurement shall be carried out in accordance with
appropriate World Bank guidelines for goods, works and services, and the thresholds specified
for the CRRP, as described in previous chapter of this document
12.26  While the Government expects that certain minimum standard procedures as described
below will be followed by the implementing agencies, as a general rule, procurement will be the
responsibility of individual implementing partners and will be carried out directly by these
agencies. Exceptions to this rule would arise only in inslances where an organization lacks
adequate experience or capacity to effectively carry out the required level of procurement, or
when obvious efficiencies may be realized. Under these rare circumstances, the specific method
and mechanisms for procurement will be highlighted in the contract between Govermnent and
implementing agency. The FMPU would be available to provide guidance and supply standard
bidding documents and formats for any specialized procurement that may be called for (such as
national competitive bidding).
12.27 Under normal circumstances, procurement under the ERSF will abide by the following
principles and thresholds:
*  All procurements under the sub-projects must be supported by appropriate
documentation, which includes specifications (or terms of reference), request for
proposal, bid, contract, invoice, and proof of payment.
* Three profonna invoices are required for procurement of any item with a unit
acquisition cost estimated to be more than Le. 100,000 (approximately $50).
*  Any service contract which is valued at more than Le. 1,500,000 (approximately
$750) must have written terms of reference (or other detailed description of expected
activities and outputs of the service) which includes minimum qualifications for



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitafion
Counhy: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 69 of87
contract award, and should be done by comparing at least three (3) qualified persons
or firms.
* Any service contract which is valued at more than Le. 25,000,000 (approximately
$12,500) must be advertised in at least two local newspapers and open to all qualified
national or international individuals or firms (as determined by the specific type of
service to be provided).
*  Contracts for civil works estimated to cost less than Le. 25,000,000 may be procured
using community participation as per guidelines to be developed by NCRRR which
meet IDA requirements
*  Contrcts for works esfimated to cost more than Le. 50,000,000 (approximately
$25,000), and for goods estimated to cost more than Le. 25,000,000 must be procured
using established National Competitive Bidding procedures (guidelines to be
provided by NCRRR).



TechnicalAnnex                       Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                   Page 70 of 87
12.28 Prior to initiating procurement under the ERSF for any infrastructure or asset to be turned
over to the community (such as a school, clinic, well, etc.), the Contractor must provide
Government with an acceptable Operations and Maintenance plan for the structure or asset that
ensures its sustainability.
Monitoring andReporting
12.29 Implementing agencies are required to submit monthly technical reports to the ERSF that
quantify project outputs and quarterly narrative progress reports providing details of project
progress and constraints. The Govermuent will identify with the implementing partner prior to
sub-project implementation the specific indicators of progress and performance for each sub-
project. These indicators will then be incorporated into the standard forms provided to the
implementing agencies by the Government so that monitoring of sub-project progress can be
uniform across similar types of activities.
12.30 Implementing agencies will submit two copies of each report to NCRRR; one will go to
the District Supervisor where the project activities are being implemented, and one to the
Regional Coordinator for the appropriate region. NCDDR will also receive a copy of the
progress report for those activities that target ex-combatants. As with the financial reports, these
technical progress reports will be submitted within 10 days of the close of the reporting period.
Customized formats for reporting technical progress will be designed and made available to
implementing partners as attachments to the standard ERSF project contract.
12.31 District Supervisors will receive the reports and be responsible for verifying the
information presented through regular communications with the implementing partners and field
visits to the project sites. The regional NCRRR Project Officers, or NCDDR Counselors and
Employment Promoters, will review and consolidate the progress reports for all active projects in
the region. The regional offices will have responsibility for entering physical progress data into a
computerized monitoring system. A consolidated report on all regional projects will be prepared
by the project officer and presented to the ERSF Projects Committee by the Regional Coordinator
for review and discussion. A copy of the same report will be sent to the relevant line ministries in
the region. The consolidated report and the electronic data will also be sent to the Directorate of
Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation (for NCRRR sub-projects), and the Monitoring and
Evaluation Manager (for NCDDR sub-projects) at national level for further consolidation and
analysis. The respective central M&E units of NCRRR and NCDDR will prepare a quarterly
progress report on the overall implementation of the ERSF.
Training and Employment Program
Overview
12.32 The Disarmament Demobilization and Reintegration of ex-combatants is an integral part
of the national peace-building process and Government's reconstruction policy as outlined in its'
Ninety-Day Program for the Restoration of Democratic Rule in Sierra Leone. The ex-combatants
constitute a considerable risk group capable of undemining current security gains and therefore
require targeted assistance. Government realizes that the security benefits of demobilization and



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 71 of 87
reintegration would, in the short-term, come at a financial cost for facilitating the return to
civilian life; yet in the long term it provides the basis for post war recovery.
12.33  Given the fact that many ex-combatants have neither finished formal education nor
acquired marketable skills, the economic outlook for many is bleak. Absorption by the formal
sector including the civil service, is beyond the reach of unskilled or functionally illiterate ex-
combatants. Therefore, the need to offer options for economic reintegration, in agriculture and
informal sector in particular, is clear so as to avoid ex-combatants reverting to rent-seeking at the
barrel of a gun.
12.34 In response, the Government has developed the Training and Employment Program
(TEP), to be coordinated by NCDDR, which will support the social and economic reintegration of
former combatants through targeted counseling, traimng and employment programs. The TEP is
a critical strategy of the overall national rehabilitation and reconstruction program that is based on
other country experiences which clearly show that the disarmament and demobilization of
combatants is only the beginning of a long and sometimes arduous process of assisting former
combatants return to their home communities and initiate productive lives.
12.35 The TEP will assist the approximately 45,000 demobilized ex-combatants of the RUF,
AFSL, AFRC and CDF currently targeted under the National DDR Program. Potential program
activities will include employment orientation workshops; short-term skills introduction/training;
information, counseling and reintegration sensitization, longer-term, market-driven skills training;
apprenticeships; non-formal literacy and numeracy education; short-term labor-intensive
employment generation activities, etc.
12.36 The Executive Secretariat of the NCDDR has been charged with the responsibility of
overall program planning and implementation of the TEP, which it will do in close collaboration
with partner organizations such as NGOs, line ministry units, parastat  organizations, training
institutions, private finns and community based organizations. Donor organizations such as the
USAID, European Union, GTZ and UNDP will support the efforts of the Executive Secretariat to
address the re-integraton needs of the former combatants.
12.37 Under the TEP,the social reintegration program would include civic education, socio-
pschological counseling and referral programs designed to prepare ex-combatants for their return
to civilian life, and to assist them by providing critical psycho-social support during the
reintegration phase.
12.38 The economic reintegration services would include a vocational orientation, counseling
on job-seeking strategies, information and counseling on training and employment opportunities
and advice on establishing micro-projects. The TEP will also offer concrete means for linking
ex-combatants to training and employment opportunities, and referrals to labor intensive public
works, broader rehabilitation projects and income generation activities.  Procedures and
implementation arrangements under the TEP would be tested during six-month pilot phase.
12.39 The TEP will also provide opportunities for vocational tamining and apprenticeships. The
program will encourage existing training suppliers to prepare and implement short modular
training courses that correspond to the educational background of the ex-combatants, their career
interests, and the needs of the labor market skills development would be provided through a
contract training scheme and training provider. In the 6-month pilot phase of the program
rehabilitation of vocational training infrastructure will also be required. Once this capacity has



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration andRehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 72 of 87
been re-established the introduction of subsidies may be provided to ensure that the training is
sufficiently demand driven and to enable ex-combatants to purchase the training that they feel
will increase their employability. The TEP will also finance training expenses incurred by
artisans who train ex-combatants through informal sector apprenticeship. Tool kits would be
provided upon commencement of the apprenticeship or after completion of the vocational training
to attract employers.
12.40 The Reintegration Unit of the NCDDR will coordinate project planning implementation
and monitoring of all TEP-supported pre-discharge orientation and reintegration assistance to ex-
combatants subsequent to discharge. The Executive Secretariat will tap into and build upon
exisfing systems and structures as well as help to build additional capacity among training
providers as necessary. TEP project proposals can be submitted to NCDDR in one of two ways:
(i) as a result of a formal Request for Proposals (REP) that would be issued by the Executive
Secretariat, or (ii) through a demand-driven process where eligible implementing agencies
identify an area of need in collaboration with ex-combatants and present an unsolicited proposal
in form and substance acceptable to NCDDR  TEP operating pnnciples, eligibility criteria for
implementing agencies, and financial requirements for proposals will be similar to those for the
ERSF. The systems and procedures to be used in implementing the TEP are described in detail in
the TEP Operations Manual.
TEPProposai Development, Review andApprovalProcedures
12.41 Requests for Proposals. Under the TEP, NCDDR will identify specific needs and
develop specific requests for proposal (RFP) for all pre-discharge orientation activities, and for
some post-discharge reintegration assistance as well. The central and regional NCDDR staff;
canp administators, and technical advisors to the Executive Secretariat will all be involved in
the identification of needs and development of specific terms of reference that will form the basis
for the RFP. In these cases, specific TORs for services will be developed, standard contracting
documents prepared, and evaluation criteria identified to be used for evaluating proposals,
negotiating final agreements, and contracting for services. The general technical criteria noted
above would be customized to each individual assignment and weightings assigned to each area
of review. Cost would also be included as an evaluation criteria. The issuing of an RFP by
NCDDR will be announced in the local newspapers and all interested and eligible organizations
will be invited to review the documentation and prepare a proposal. Standard World Bank
guidelines for quality and cost-based selection of consultant services will be used by NCDDR for
these types of procurement. Detailed technical and financial review of proposals will be carried
out by a specific evaluation committee formed at the time an RFP is issued.
12.42 In certain instances, when the anticipated size of a contract is below US$100,000, and
when speed of response is of the essence, Government may opt to send an RFP directly to a short-
list of eligible organizations who have been identified earlier and pre-qualified to carry out certain
type and size activities. In this case, a short-list of no fewer than three and no more than six
organizations or training institutions will be invited to bid on a specific assigmnent required by
NCDDR. Organizations will be eligible to apply and be pre-qualified to bid on such assignments
at any time during the implementation of the Project. Short-listed firms will be selected randomly
from the most recently updated list of pre-qualified organizations.
12.43 World Bank no-objection will be required prior to the issuance of any RFPs and the
signature of any contract with an estimated value of US$100,000 or more for a firm, or



TechnicalAnnex                      Project itie: CommunityReintegration andRehabilitafion
Counhy: Sierra Leone                                                   Page 73 of 87
US$50,000 or more for an individual. Furthermore, Bank non-objection will be required for all
TORs and for any single source contract issued by NCDDR for TEP related assistance, regardless
of contract value.
12.44 Unsolicted ProposaL NCDDR also expects that certain reintegration support projects
will result from a demand-oriented process whereby ex-combatants and eligible implementing
agencies together formulate an assistance proposal. These proposals might include micro-credit
assistance, small-scale public works activities, business management taining, or provide for start-
up capital, etc. In this case, the procedures, formats and guidelines developed for NCRRR's
Emergency Recovery Support Fund (ERSF) will be adapted and applied to the TEP. These
formats and procedures have been designed to help standardize proposal submissions and review,
and are presented and described in detail in the ERSF Operations Manual. Predefined general
and specific technical criteria would be applied to each unsolicited proposal, similar to the
procedure under the ERSF. Particular attention would be given to: a) the degree to which the
proposal reflects the expressed need of the proposed group of ex-combatants, and b) the capacity
of the implementing agency to cafry out the proposed assistance. If necessary, NCDDR staff
would seek more specific technical input from relevant line ministries or public agencies
(for instance, from SLRA in the case that a road works project is submitted) in assessing these
types of proposals.
12.45 Under World Bank financed TEP activities, unsolicited proposals would require a
10 percent counter-part contribution from either the beneficiary group, the implementing partner,
or the Government. The World Bank would reimburse up to a maximnm of 90 percent of the
otal value of a TEP financed activity resulting from an unsolicited proposal.
12.46  Contactng. In the case of RFPs, final project proposals will be negotiated according to
the procedures laid out in the World Bank guidelines for the selection and employment of
consulans (January 1997, revised September 1997 and lanuary 1999). Once successfully
negotiated, contracts will be signed between implementing agencies and the Executive Secretauy
of NCDDR for defined services. NCDDR will use the standard consultant service contract for
fixed sum or time-based assignments for contracts resulting from an RFP. For unsolicited
proposals, NCDDR will use either the approved contract between implementing agencies and the
GOSL (as shown in the existing Operations Manual for the ERSF), or standard World Bank
contracts for finns.
Disbursement and Financial Control under TEP
12.47 All disbursement and financial control responsibilities under TEP will be managed by the
Financial Management and Procurement Unit. This unit will be responsible for disbursing funds
to all consultants, contractors, and vendors on behalf of NCDDR (as well as NCRRR) once
authorization to do so is given by the respective institution. The FMPU will maintain all financial
records (including contacts, invoices, financial reports, etc.) related to TEP activities, ensure that
contractors abide by the financial management and procurement requirements under the Project,
and prepare all necessary financial reports, requests for payments, etc. required by the World
Bank and other donors hat support the TEP and the NDDRP. The FMPU will also take
responsibility for assisting NCDDR with procurement of necessary inputs for the overall Project,
and, as necessary, assist contractors to procure goods that may be required under the TEP assisted
activities. Finally, the FMPU will ensure that the internal control procedures for the use of CRRP
resources (as applied under the TEP as well as other components of the Project) are adhered to



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration andRehabilitafion
County: Sierra Leone                                                 Page 74 of 87
and that agreed upon review, approval and contracting procedures for the TEP as outlined in this
procedures manual are maintained.
12.48 Once a contract is signed between a service provider and the Government, the FMPU will
take responsibility for the disbursement of funds so that work can be carried out. Contractors will
be required to submit monthly statements of expenses and periodic- depending on the type and
schedule of assistance being carried out- requests for reimbursement. NCDDR staff, either the
regional finance officers, site coordiats, or administator will review these SOEs and
reimbursement requests to ensure that reported expenses are reasonable and match reported
physical progress being made under the sub-project. Once approved by NCDDR staff these
financial reports will be submitted to the FMPU where expenditures will be checked for
eligibility, and subsequent payments made based the sub-project budget, current expenditure
category balances, and the disbursement schedule. The FMPU will retain all financial records
associated with the TEP supported activities and would therefore be the focus of both donor
supervision mission review and semi-annual Project audits. As a part of the Project audit,
a random sampling of implementing agency sub-projects will be reviewed in detail to assess
adherence to both the financial management and contractual obligations under the TEP.
12.49 The standard procedures for procurement, monitoring, financial and technical progress
reporting for implementing agencies under the TEP are similar to those required under the ERSF.
Specialized Delivery Procedures under the ERSF
12.50 Public Works Social Safety Nd. A social safety net program that uses labor-intensive
public works activities to create temporary employment among vulnerable groups of ex-
combatants, unemployed urban youth, newly retuming rural communities, and others such as
female-headed households will be implemented as a distinct component under the ERSF. An
outline of the key issues to be considered in targeting and implementing this component is
presented in Attachment 13.
12.51 After consideration of alternative options, the Government and the World Bank
concluded that the ERSF sub-project review and approval procedures should be used for this
element of the Project. The same staff and organizational sucures are capable of handling these
types of projects, and the inter-agency review process will help guarantee both technical
suitability of sub-projects and coordination with other similar activities (such as food for work or
food for agriculture projects). The recent revisions to the institutional structure of NCRRR that
include adding a Western Area office and seeking civil engineers for district level posts, will
facilitate the inclusion of public works sub-project proposals in the review process. Specific
technical criteria will be incorporated into the Project's Operational Manual and be used in
reviewing and assessing these types of public works activities. Based on World Bank best
practices for similar programs, the suggested criteria are as follows:
* public works projects should create temporary employment among the most vulnerable,
while at the same time optimize the social, economic and/or physical infrastructure of the
community;, ideally, being oriented toward funther job creation and/or income
generation;
* geographic targeting of projects to help ensure greatest participation of poorest; therefore,
urban projects should be in those areas where there is general agreement that the poorest
live (e.g.,., Goderich, Congo Town, Dworzak Farm, Kroobay, Ginger Hall, and large



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
County: Sierra Leone                                                  Page 75 of87
parts of Kissy and Wellington, etc., for Freetown) and for recently returned communities
(within last 12 months) for rural areas and projects;
* project wage rate not to exceed the prevailing market wage for unskilled labor. For
example, in mid-1998, the average daily wage rate for unskilled labor was approximately
Le 1,500, or US$1 per day;
* the total wage component for unskilled labor of the public works project should, on
average, be equal to or exceed 50 percent of the total project cost;
* depending on the type of public works and the location, wages could be paid in both cash
and in-kind (eg, food), however, the total value should not exceed a daily wage rate of
Le. 1,500 as discussed above (per discussions between SLRA and NGOs suggest that for
the implementation of all food-aid projects, 30% of the labor cost shall be paid in food
and 70% in cash);
* projects should encourage linkages between work and productivity through incorporating
a piece-rate or task-based payment system for laborers. (for instances, SLRA's Feeder
Roads Department has established wage rates per man-day for 18 different road
rehabilitation activities);
* projects need to be consistent with existing labor laws;
* projects need to be technically feasible and proposals must demonstrate that appropriate
technical norms and design standards wil be adhered to;
* guarantee of community participation in project design, execution, and maintenance to
ensure long-term sustainability;
* timing and design of services/works should, to the extent possible, encourage broadest
participation and reduce opportumty costs of participation through seasonal timing
(i.e., not during the planting or harvesting seasons) and flexible working arrangements
(such as piece meal rates).
* numnber of laborers at any one-job site should range between 50 and 75, and not exceed
100.
12.52 Pmbfic Sector Instiutions. The technical departments, regional, and district offices of
the line ministries of the GOSL have an important role to fulfill in the implementation of the
ERSF. First, relevant line ministries (i.e., health, education, agriculture, works, etc.) are al
potential implementing partners to the ERSF. NCRRR fully expects that the appropriate
technical units and offices of these ministries will be active participants in the development and
implementation of sub-projects. Secondly, the technical ministries will play a critical role at the
regional level in providing guidance and input to the ERSF project staff and Projects Committee.
NCRRR recognizes the technical expertise and responsibility that the line ministries have in
guaranteeing adherence to sectoral standards and norms. Therefore, technical sub-committees at
the regional level will be called upon to provide specialized knowledge and advice on given sub-
project proposals. The senior technical officer from the relevant line ministry or parastatal
agency will be the contact person for the NCRRR in order to coordinate appropriate technical
reviews of ERSF sub-project proposals. NCRRR will make available to sub-committee members
or specified technical personnel in the regions adequate logistical support to facilitate these
technical reviews of proposals. Finally, NCRRR will rely on the line ministry departments to
help monitor sub-project implementation-- especially regarding technical specifications and
norms. District level NCRRR staff responsible for monitoring ERSF sub-project implementation



TechnicalAnnex                      Project Title: Community Reintegration and Rehabilitatfion
County: &erra Leone                                                   Page 76 of 87
will be responsible for securing the close coordination of line ministry personnel at the same level
when specific technical matters of project implementation are being considered.
12.53 Given the special nature of the line ministry departments as the coordinator of all national
activities for their respective sectors, and as the regulatng body in terms of technical norms and
procedures, the process whereby the line ministries apply to and are financed under the ERSF will
vary slightly. First any project submitted by a district or regional line ministry office must be
endorsed by the appropriate central ministry department. Second, in the event of a national level
project activity (such as for an information campaign, staff traioing etc.), the proposal to the
ERSF would be submitted direy to NCRRR at the central level, and would be reviewed by the
National ERSF Projects Committee. Third, fund disbursement for activities implemented through
line ministry departments would have to be consistent with the financial management
requirements of the Government, as well as abide by the procedures of the FMPU.



TechnicalAnnex                     Project Title: Community Reintegration andRehabilitation
Country: Sierra Leone                                                Page 77 of 87
13. FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
Introduction
13.1  The CRRP has two principal components, namely: (i) an Emergency Recovery Support
Fund (ERSF), and related institutional support to NCRRR and partners; and (ii) a Training and
Employment Program (TEP), and related institutional support to NCDDR. Both components will
finance sub-project activities through a social fundtype mechanism. The ERSF is designed to
finance demand-driven community based sub-projects, while the TEP is designed to finance the
social and economic reintegration of former combatants. The Government initiated the CRRP in
1997 with funding from the Project Preparation Facility. The objective of the Project is to
stimulate economic recovery by expediting the reintegration of internally displaced persons, ex-
combatants and refugees and restoring the provision of basic social services.
Analysis of Alternatives
13.2  The Project has been developed by the key stakeholders in a participatory manner. As
the Government's implementation capacity has been weakened considerably in the course of the
conflict, it was considered advisable for the Project to put greater emphasis on implementation
through existing structures and capacities of partner organizaions: i.e. NGOs, existing project
units and, where feasible, line ministries. During the emergency period, Government has
successfully developed effective coordination mechanisms at national and regional level that will
be used to mobilize these implementing partners to better ensure effective coordination.
Atetive Project implementation strategies considered included implementation via line
ministries or through the exclusive use of technical assistance. Both options were rejected; the
first because of capacity constraints and the second due to concerns regarding ownership,
community participation, and sustanability.
13.3  The capacity of the implementing parters, in particular NGOs, to deliver services has
been analyzed using a number of proxy indicators such as the number of organizations, the time
they have been operatig in country, their sectoral and geographic areas of operation, and their
highest recorded annual program value in the lst five years. Sierra Leone has had a well
established community of developmental NGOs which has further expanded in response to the
war-related breakdown in government capacity. About 71 percent of development organizations
operating today have started to work after 1990.
13.4  The implementation capacity of international NGOs by far exceeds the capacity of
national NGOs, taking the highest annual program value during the Lst five years as an indcator.
International NGOs have an annual turnover of about US$80 million, while the program value of
national NGOs amounts to only US$5.4 million.16 The CRRP will provide institutional support
to national NGOs to enhance their capacity to plan and implement sub-projects.
16 Based on a study conducted in mid-1997.
