Consultancy to Design the Monitoring and Evaluation System
for the Government of Sierra Leone
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
The signing of the Lome Peace Agreement in July 1999 brought a spirit of hope to the
people of Sierra Leone. Government and the people now look forward to moving the
country from a period of emergency to one of reconstruction and resettlement. The
implementation of the long-delayed socio-economic recovery programme should assist
with the consolidation of the peace and laying the foundations for sustained economic
growth. To this end, the Commission eagerly looks forward to kick starting the NRRRP
which was abrogated by political instability which has was predicated by civil conflict.
Given the fact Government is in partnership with other stakeholders and implementing
partners, it is a necessary condition that a robust but effective and flexible monitoring
and evaluation system is set up that would be economy-wide, but embraces the various
compartments or sub-programmes/projects of the other stakeholders and implementing
partners. This system would harmonise all the other sub-systems and generate a
common data- base from which the entire spectrum of assistance to the country's
recovery programme can be evaluated
The NCRRR and its partner agencies recognise the need for an effective and holistic
monitoring and evaluation system that will provide accurate and timely information on
the entire economic and social recovery programmes and sub-projects that are being
implemented countrywide. This should enable the making of appropriate and timely
management decisions. It is in this context that this consultancy is required
OBJECTIVE
Working with the Director of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation as the National
Counterpart, the consultant will assist the Commission to develop a comprehensive M &
E system for the NRRP which will take into account all the sub-programmes being
implemented by the various partners of the Government in the socio-economic recovery
process.
The system to be developed should provide senior management with an effective tool
to:
* Provide a bird's eye view of the entire NRRP at a glance;
* Assess the administrative performance of the central and regional levels of the
NCRRR;



Technical Annex                             Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                           Attachment 10: Page 2 of 3
* Provide information on the progress and constraints affecting the implementation of
programmes and sub-projects in order to be able to take appropriate and timely
action;
* Provide on a regular basis and on special requests a concise status and analysis of
RRR activities;
* Analyse the impact of programme activities as implemented by RRR partner
organisations in terms of their contribution to rehabilitation, reconstruction and
poverty reduction.
Specifically, the consultant will:
* Analyse all the donor-supported RRR programmes and projects (CRRP,
CRRP/ADB, EAP, PDP, and any other) with a specific focus on extracting from
these specific objectives for the programme's components, activities, inputs and
outputs related to these;
* Based on the programme and individual project components, develop the framework
and quantitative indicators for the overall programme performance as regards to:
i)   overall funding of programme
ii)  institutional capacity of NCRRR to manage programmes;
iii)  individual project inputs, outputs and outcomes
iv)   service delivery performance including a measure for effectiveness of the ERSF
and other sub-project review, approval and disbursements of funds
v)   ensuring an equitable balance in approved sub-projects in terms of geographical
distribution, priority sectors and especially vulnerable groups such as women
and ex-combatants
t Examine existing and proposed reporting procedures and formats and recommend,
when relevant, revisions and additions necessary for the satisfactory performance of
the M&E system.
* Develop a strategy for the computerisation of M&E information taking into account
the existing conditions and human resource capacity;
* Based on the computerisation strategy, develop an implementation plan for setting
up a computerized database using standard software (Microsoft Windows 98
compatible).
* Determine related staff requirements in terms of number and minimum skill
requirements.
* Design a suitable database structure for monitoring of RRR activities, including
standard auto-generated overview reports.
* Recommend on computers, software and peripherals required for the operations of
the M&E Unit of the NCRRR.
* Prepare detailed Terms of Reference for a national System Analyst/Programmer to
develop the database application and supervise the System Analyst/Programmer in
developing NCRRR M&E Database(s).
* Liaise with the M&E Unit and the System Analyst/programmer of NCDDR and other

Sierra Leone                                             Attachment 10: Page 3 of 3
partners to determine the appropriateness and requirements of inter-agency data
linkages.
* Train counterpart staff in the use of the M&E database system.
* Build the capacity of NCRRR and other partners in monitoring and evaluation
techniques and procedures;
* Prepare an Operations Guide on Monitoring and Evaluation Procedures.
* Prepare a Data Dictionary on the Monitoring and Evaluation Database.
* Recommend methods to monitor beneficiary level impact of program activities and

OVERVIEW OF THE EMERGENCY RECOVERY SUPPORT FUND
(REVISED AUGUST 1998)
1. Overview
The Emergency Recovery Support Fund (ERSF) is a Government of Sierra Leone initiative co-ordinated
by the National Commission for Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation (NCRRR) to help
address the negative social and economic affects of more than seven years of civil war and ten months of
junta rule. The Commission has been charged with the responsibility of helping rebuild the country and
will do so in collaboration with key donor and implementing partner organizations. Several donor
organizations, including the World Bank, the African Development Bank, and the UNDP, will support the
efforts of the Commission to address the urgent needs of resettling displaced people and refugees,
restoring basic social services and infrastructure in the war affected areas, reintegrating ex-combatants,
and addressing the needs of peace building and reconciliation. The ERSF will also support a labor-
intensive public works program designed to assist the poorest members of society in the principal urban
centers.
The ERSF will finance community-based projects, which address the priority needs of internally
displaced persons, refugees, former combatants, and vulnerable groups in urban centers. The Fund will
be used to address multi-sectoral needs; starting with more urgent food production and temporary shelter,
and running through medium and longer term concerns for basic infrastructure repair and rehabilitation of
productive assets. These projects will be implemented by eligible partners who include international and
national NGOs, government line ministries, existing donor project units, and qualified community-based
organizations.
The Commission and the ERSF can only begin to support the long process of rebuilding the country;
other funding and implementation strategies will be necessary to address the overall needs for
reconstruction and rehabilitation. As the country moves from an emergency recovery phase to a longer-
term developmental one, more strategic sectoral investment programs implemented through the
respective line ministries and implementing partners will be required.  As well as sharing this
responsibility for helping rebuild the country with line ministries and implementing partners, the ERSF
must also serve to catalyze the self-help efforts of each village. In the end, the local population must take
the initiative and responsibility for rebuilding the individual lives, community infrastructure, and social
ties negatively affected by the war and unrest.
The Fund will rely on a decentralized bottom-up project planning, approval and monitoring process,
which taps into and builds upon existing systems and structures at the regional and local levels. NGOs,
line ministries and other entities will be called upon to design sub-projects in close collaboration with
village, section and chiefdom authorities. Regional NCRRR staff will have primary responsibility for
day-to-day management of the project review process. Regional committees will be formed and
composed of two NCRRR staff members, a Ministry of Finance, Development and Economic Planning
representative, the regional HACU field officer, and one representative of the implementing partners to
be selected by the Inter-agency Forum. These regional Projects Committees will have the responsibility
and authority to approve projects up to a maximum given level. NCRRR headquarters' staff will provide
the broad policy and program-planning framework for the ERSF, monitor its implementation and co-
ordinate the overall process.



Technical Annex                                     Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                     Attachment II: Page 2 of 13
The ERSF has been designed through a process of consultation, investigation, and consensus building
initiated by the then Ministry of National Reconstruction, Rehabilitation and Resettlement, and others, in
mid-1996. Over a ten-month period immediately preceding the coup, MNRRR staff, central and regional
government authorities, national and international NGOs, and key international agencies and donors
involved in the rehabilitation and recovery process were consulted on how best such a funding
mechanism should work. This collaborative process culminated in two stakeholder workshops to
elaborate and finalize these procedures with Ministry staff, NGO representatives, HACU and the World
Bank held in November 1996 and May 1997. The workshop participants identified nine principles,
which should underpin both the design and implementation of the ERSF. These are:
1. the ERSF and the National Resettlement, Reconstruction and Rehabilitation Program (NRRRP) is
operating in a dynamic and changing environment-- therefore, the Fund's operation systems must be
flexible and open to revision as the situation on the ground and in the provinces also changes,
2. given the fluid nature of the program environment, phasing of different aspects of the Fund, and its
administrative systems, must be considered,
3. the Fund's administration should be decentralized and have a regional focus,
4. the Fund should tie into existing systems and structures; be coordinated and integrated at provincial
levels,
5. the project review and monitoring process must be open and transparent,
6. the systems must operate quickly without sacrificing accountability,
7. the Fund should support productive activities,
8. the Fund should emphasize community and stakeholder participation, and
9. the administrative procedures should not adversely affect the existing relationships among NGOs, or
between NGOs and the communities with which they work.
The consultative process used to arrive at these procedures created a shared vision of how the ERSF
should work, and a shared responsibility for its successful implementation among the Commission and its
implementing partners. This spirit of partnership and collaboration should be maintained as the Fund
begins operating. If the systems and procedures do not function as envisioned then it should be seen as
the joint responsibility of all parties to ensure that a new system be put in place that works. Furthermore,
subsequent reviews and revisions of these systems should draw upon these same collaborative
mechanisms and co-operation to devise ways of better implementing the program as experience is gained.
Specifically, NCRRR will conduct a detailed review of the ERSF procedures and projects six months
after the fund is fully operational (expected to be October, 1998) in order to test the effectiveness of the
procedures proposed herein. The review will look at, inter alia, the effectiveness of the project review
process, the membership of the approval committees, the level of project approval authority at regional
level, and community representation in the overall process.
This document serves as both an overview of the ERSF for donors and other interested parties, and as
guidelines to potential implementing partners for the preparation of project proposals for consideration by
the NCRRR. The document is divided into four subsequent sections that deal with the overall objectives
of the ERSF, evaluation criteria and the project selection process, administration of funds, and procedures
for project monitoring. A more detailed Operational Manual has also been prepared for use by NCRRR
staff in overseeing the implementation of the ERSF.



Technical Annex                                        Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                        Attachment 11: Page 3 of 13
2. Objectives and Principles of the ERSF
The objectives of the ERSF are:
*  To assist communities and vulnerable social groups in re-establishing their livelihood and
production systems;
*  To help rehabilitate community level infrastructure (roads, schools, clinics, water systems, court
barries, rural markets, irrigation systems) damaged during the conflict and in the poorest sections
of Freetown and other urban centres, in accordance with needs and priorities as expressed by
communities themselves;
*  To support the re-establishment of basic service provision in the fields of health, education,
agriculture, etc. in areas affected by the conflict;
*  To provide support to local-level initiatives aimed at reconciliation and rebuilding social
structures damaged during the years of the conflict; and
*  To strengthen the capacity of local organisations to provide effective support to longer-term
processes of reconstruction and development.
Projects qualifying for support under the ERSF should be developed and implemented in accordance with
the following principles:
1. Enhance the opportunities for self-reliance of displaced and affected communities;
2. Strengthen local capacity to implement and manage reconstruction and development activities;
3. Promote awareness and sensitivity for gender equity and the protection of the specially
disadvantaged (e.g., children, widows and the disabled) in the decision-making process;
4. Maximize grassroots participation and input in the project cycle;
5. Promote an integrated approach to resettlement and rehabilitation; and
6. Build the objectives of maintaining peace, reconciliation andforgiveness into the resettlement
process.
3. Basic Eligibility Criteria
To be eligible for funding under the ERSF, implementing organizations and the project must meet certain
basic criteria. These criteria are sub-divided between institutional, sectoral, geographic, target group and
financial, as discussed below.
3.1 Criteria for Implementing Partners
For an organization to be eligible for funding under the ERSF it must meet the following organizational
criteria:
* be a legal entity registered in Sierra Leone and able to enter into a legally binding contract;
* have a minimum of 2 years working experience (within or outside Sierra Leone)
implementing the same or similar activities as those proposed to the ERSF;
* have adequate infrastructure and personnel currently based in the region of proposed activity
to carry out the project;



Technical Annex                                       Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                       Attachment II: Page 4 of 13
*   have adequate administrative and financial management systems and procedures in place to
safe guard the use of ERSF resources; and
*   have no legal dispute or debts which would materially affect the institution's ability to
undertake the project.
3.2 Project Types
In line with the overall objectives of the National Resettlement, Reconstruction and Rehabilitation
Program, the ERSF will support only those projects that address the following broad sectors and areas of
activity:
*  Agricultural Rehabilitation: including support to basic productive activities primarily
through the provision of seeds, tools and emergency food supply (using existing food
pipelines) for the farming season; support to rehabilitation of tree crops, other cash crops,
fishing (through the provision of equipment) and animal husbandry (through re-stocking of
animals and provision of veterinary drugs);
*   Shelter: principally to provide temporary shelter and provide the planning framework for
permanent facilities;
* Rehabilitation of Community Infrastructure: to support the rehabilitation of schools, health
centres, court barries, water supply, roads, rural market-places and other priorities as
identified by communities;
*   Re-establishment of Essential Services: to provide human and material resources essential to
the re-establishment of basic health and education services in collaboration with the relevant
ministries;
*   Reintegration of Vulnerable Groups: supporting the reintegration of specific groups (e.g.,
widows, female head of households, ex-combatants, children affected by the war and
disabled people) into communities; which may include temporary safety nets, vocational
training, capital/credit, counselling/trauma healing, information, employment (e.g. labour-
intensive public works), education and/or functional literacy;
*   Promotion of Reconciliation: for programs promoting reconciliation and conflict resolution
at the community level; which may include developing conflict resolution skills, promoting
reconciliation through local media, supporting youth, community and voluntary associations
with the potential for promoting reconciliation;
*   Jobs Creation through Public Works: to establish a temporary social safety net mechanism
targeting vulnerable groups of unemployed and ex-combatants living and working in urban
((Freetown and provincial capitals) and rural areas; labour-intensive works will be supported
in environmental sanitation and waste disposal, road repair and maintenance, public building
maintenance, reforestation, etc.
*   Small Enterprise Promotion and Vocational Training Schemes: designed to support
development of productive skills among individual target group members and generating
income and employment at community level; and
*   Capacity-Building: of local organisations in all of the above areas the development.
3.3 Geographic Focus
The ERSF will be active throughout the regions and districts of Sierra Leone that has been affected by the
war. Priority will be given to supporting populations returning to rural areas and for those areas most
severely affected by the war. The Fund will also support labor-intensive public works activities that
target the most vulnerable groups of unemployed in the urban and rural areas.



Technical Annex                                     Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                    Attachment 11: Page 5 of 13
The ERSF will co-ordinate with other donor recovery activities (e.g., EU Resettlement and Rehabilitation
Program, line ministry emergency plans, NGO initiatives, etc.) so as to avoid duplication and overlap in
their areas of operation. The procedures and mechanisms for project review and approval will also
support the funding mechanism of the Government's Social Action and Poverty Alleviation (SAPA)
Program, which is located within NCRRR.
3.4 Target Groups
To be eligible for consideration, any project proposal presented to the ERSE must target the following
beneficiary groups:
* internally displaced persons and refugees who left their homes and communities due to the
war and/or subsequent civil strife that resulted from junta rule and its aftermath;
*   people in war-affected villages who never moved but are taking initiatives to re-establish
themselves and their communities as well as people returning spontaneously to their villages
from displaced camps, bush camps and other areas;
*   vulnerable groups, such as female headed households; children affected by the conflict
through war trauma, being orphaned, or disrupted schooling; and disabled people, including
those severely injured or traumatised by the war;
e ex-combatants and their dependants; and
* urban unemployed living in the poorest areas of Freetown, Makeni, Bo, and Kenema.
3.5 Financial Restrictions
The following budget restrictions and financial guidelines will govern ERSF projects:
*  no project budget and supported activities should exceed 12 months duration, nor go beyond
the 3-year mandate of the Commission;
*   a maximum funding limit of approximately Le.850,000,000 (US$500,000) per project will
apply;
*   at any given time, the ERSF will not provide funding to an organisation in excess of 50% of
the organisation's previous year's annual budget;
*   only allowable cost categories (as defined in the proposal development guidelines) will be
considered for financing under the ERSF;
*  no counter-part funds are required of implementing partners; however, community in-kind
contributions will be encouraged for community infrastructure projects (clinics, schools,
etc.);
* up to a maximum of 20% of the total ERSF proposed project budget will be allowed for
"organisational" costs including administrative costs, overheads, general equipment which
would not directly benefit the community, etc.; and
* purchase of large assets such as institutional infrastructure, vehicles, etc. will be
discouraged, and the disposition of any vehicles upon project completion will be the subject
of negotiation between NCRRR and the implementing partner.
4. Technical Evaluation Criteria
Once a project and organization are shown to meet the basic eligibility criteria listed above, projects
submitted to the ERSF will be reviewed and evaluated based on two sets of technical criteria, general and
specific.



Technical Annex                                           Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                            Attachment 11: Page 6 of l3
4.1 General Criteria
General criteria will be applied to each project proposal by the ERSF secretariat staff (see sections 5.1
and 5.2) in assessing overall project design and fundability. This assessment would look at institutional,
financial, social and environmental criteria, project coherence, and sustainability. These categories of
criteria are described in more detail below.
Institutional criteria; including number and types of personnel, available infrastructure and physical
resources to carry out project, financial management and administrative systems, years and types of
experience, project value relative to overall organizational finances, etc.
Project coherence; including relation between identified problems, causes and proposed solutions, the
clarity and specificity of project objectives and links to proposed project activities, feasibility of
achieving the proposed end results, completeness and reasonability of the monitoring plan, and the
innovativeness or creativity of the project design.
Financial criteria; including a clear relation between project activities and budget items,
reasonableness of proposed inputs and unit costs, inputs conform with standard specifications and
applicable market rates, budget form and substance in agreement with guidelines (no unacceptable
expenditure categories, adequate information, etc.), and the relative level of "direct" and "indirect"
costs.
Sustainability criteria; including level and substance of community participation in project planning
and implementation, level of local knowledge or skills required for implementation, level of local
labor and/or materials in project, level of operation or maintenance costs once project is complete,
viability of maintenance plan, impact on disposable time of women, income generating potential of
project, and effect project has on environment and natural resources.
Social criteria; including understanding of ethnic characteristics of community and region, impact on
vulnerable groups, and potential conflicts or obstacles between different groups or institutions within
community.
Environmental criteria; including identification and mitigation of potential environmental impacts of
the proposed projects, in particular issues related to health and sanitation, natural resource
exploitation and pesticide use.
This review will be carried out by ERSF/NCRRR staff in the offices and at the proposed sites of the
implementing partners. Clearly, the larger, more complex and more costly the project, the more detailed
and thorough the general technical review will be. In particular, the regional and district level staff of
NCRRR should assess the extent to which the project reflects the priorities of the community in which the
project will be implemented.
4.2 Specific Technical Criteria
Suitable line ministries and technical sub-committees (as appropriate) in each region will conduct specific
technical evaluations of each proposal. It will be the responsibility of these groups to apply appropriate
technical criteria to the project review; such criteria will vary depending on the project type (i.e.,
agriculture, infrastructure, basic services, or enterprise development). In general, however, these sub-
committees or task forces would look at the following areas in making a technical assessment of the
project:



Technical Annex                                       Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                       Attachment 11: Page 7 of 13
*   demonstrated understanding of the factors and technical conditions influencing the project
and beneficiary groups;
*   conformity with standard technical specifications, norms or practices for the particular sector
under consideration (such as road specifications, standard building designs, or health
treatment protocols);
*   technical soundness and adequacy of the proposed solutions (i.e., will the problem be
resolved with the level and extent of input and assistance proposed), and if the project
objectives are realistic and achievable;
*   technical skills, knowledge and capacity of implementing agency/staff; and
*   use of proven and appropriate technical practices or methods.
5. ERSF Structures and Procedures
Administration of the ERSF is distinguished by two important features; a decentralized management
structure, and a shared responsibility between NCRRR and its implementing partners (NGOs, line
ministries, etc.) in both the administration and implementation of the Fund.
The NCRRR has final responsibility for the ERSF and reports to its funding agencies on overall resource
use and achievements under the Fund. Implementing partners contracted by NCRRR and working at the
local level will carry out day-to-day implementation of project activities. These partners report directly to
district and regional offtces of NCRRR, which will monitor the Fund. An inter-agency ERSF Projects
Committee will be established in each provincial capital to review, approve, or recommend all ERSF
projects proposed for that region. A central level ERSF Projects Committee will also be set up to review
all projects exceeding the regional project approval threshold of US$50,000.  The central level
Committee will also take responsibility for approving and monitoring all Western Area project proposals.
District Supervisors and Community Development Officers will serve as outreach staff for the
Commission at the district level. These staff will be responsible for co-ordination and information
exchange with district-level government representatives (especially line ministries); facilitating NCRRR
linkages with village leaders, section heads and paramount chiefs; encouraging the participation of local
groups and communities in the ERSF; and monitoring the work of implementing partners.
The central-level Commission will have responsibility for overall financial management of the ERSF,
monitoring and evaluation of ERSF and NRRRP implementation, setting and revising ERSF policy, and
co-ordination of implementing partners at the national level.
5.1 Relevant ERSF Structures
There are six structures, four at regional levels and two at the center, which will share the responsibility
for the successful implementation of the Fund. These structures are (i) the regional ERSF Projects
Committee, (ii) the ERSF technical secretariat within the Regional NCRRR office, (iii) the regional Inter-
agency Forum, (iv) the technical committees or task forces operating in each region, (v) the National
ERSF Projects Committee, and (vi) the Financial Management and Procurement Unit. Each body and its
overall responsibilities are described below.
The ERSF Projects Committee will be established and given the responsibility for reviewing and
approving projects up to the threshold level of $US 50,000, equivalent.1 This Committee will be
During the pilot phase of the ERSF (the first six-months of operation), all projects above US$25,000 equivalent will also
require the approval of the National ERSF Projects Committee in Freetown.



Technical Annex                                           Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                            Attachment II: Page 8 of 13
composed of 2 NCRRR staff (the Regional Co-ordinator and one other staff member2) the HACU
regional officer, the regional MOFDEP representative, and one representative of the implementing
partners who will be nominated by the Inter-agency Forum.3 The Regional Co-ordinator will chair the
Committee.4
The ERSF technical secretariat, consisting of two projects officers and one finance officer, will be set up
in each region and will be responsible for the day-to-day administrative tasks associated with ERSF
project applications, reviews, and approvals. While the secretariat is responsible to. the Projects
Committee, on a day-to-day basis these staff will report to the NCRRR Regional Co-ordinator.
The Regional Inter-agency Forum will provide broad-based, multi-sectoral and inter-agency feedback to
the project approval and monitoring process. This body is chaired by the NCRRR Regional Co-ordinator
and serves as the forum for general information exchange and identification of potential problems or
conflicts in ERSF project design and implementation. All proposed projects will be presented to this
body before detailed review by NCRRR staff and the appropriate technical body so as to draw upon the
collective knowledge of the broader development community in the regions. Membership in this forum is
open to all interested parties and potential implementing partners throughout the region.
Technical Sub-committees. As neither the ERSF Projects Committee nor the technical secretariat will
have all the information or technical know-how to assess all types of proposals, existing, or newly
formed, technical sub-committees or task-forces5 will be called upon to review specific technical aspects
of project proposals. The NCRRR Regional Co-ordinator will seek the cooperation of the relevant
technical committees or ad hoc groups through the appropriate line ministry or public authority (such as
SLRA), and will have access to necessary resources to facilitate individual and committee members input
to this process.
A National ERSF Projects Committee will be formed at central level to: (i) make recommendations on all
sub-project approvals for contracts which exceed the regional approval threshold of US$50,000, (ii)
review and approve all urban public works projects that target the unemployed of Greater Freetown, and
(iii) review and approve all project proposals that are national in scope such as those presented by central
level line ministries. The National Committee will be chaired by the NCRRR Director for RRR, and will
include the Director for Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation; the Director of the SAPA Program; a
representative for the Development Department of MOFDEP; a representative of UN/OCHA, and one
representative of the donors that support the ERSF.
The Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU) will be established to assist NCRRR with all
aspects of contracting, disbursements, financial management and reporting in relation to the ERSF, as
well as other donor financed activities within the Commission. Once an ERSF project is approved and
contracts are signed by the Commission, the FMPU will manage all direct payments made to
implementing partners and will ensure that financial reporting and record keeping is in line with GOSL
and donor requirements, and internationally accepted accounting standards. The table below indicates the
main characteristics and responsibilities of these ERSF structures.

5.2 Project Review & Approval Procedures
Project Development. Potential implementing partners should first contact NCRRR to receive appropriate
guidance in preparing a proposal. For this purpose, NCRRR has developed guidelines and standard
formats to assist implementing partners in the proposal development process. While information will be
available at all NCRRR central, regional, and district offices, partners are encouraged to contact the
appropriate regional or district levels office nearest the point where project will be implemented.
Potential implementing partners will present their project idea, or concept, to the Inter-agency Forum for
review and comment prior to submission of a full proposal. This review is designed to ensure better co-
ordination of development activities in the region, and to identify any potential problems or concerns in
the design of the project. The implementing partner will be encouraged to present the project concept to
the IAF directly, however, if an organization cannot be present at the IAF meeting, NCRRR staff (either



Technical Annex                                       Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                     Attachment 11: Page 10 of 13
district or regional) can present the project concept on behalf of the implementing partner. NCRRR will
develop a standard form that implementing partners can use to prepare and submit this brief project
summary to the ERSF project officer at the regional NCRRR office. The project officer would then
determine when the summary would be presented to the IAF based on the volume and backlog of
proposals, and the other duties of the IAF.
A project concept must be reviewed by the L4F before it can be consideredfor funding by NCRRR
Once the IAF has reviewed and commented upon a project idea, an implementing partner can present a
full project proposal to NCRRR taking into consideration any comments or concerns raised by the 1AF.
Project proposals should be submitted directly to the NCRRR regional office, however, district and
chiefdom based organizations might submit proposals to NCRRR through the district offices if more
convenient. District staff will have no direct responsibility for reviewing proposals, however, and will
transmit all proposals (and project concepts) directly to the regional office.
An NGO or CBO mustfill out an "NGO Profile"form and submit it to NCRRR before it is eligiblefor
funding under the ERSF.
Proposal Review. Project proposal review will be the main responsibility of the ERSF secretariat staff
(the Projects and Finance officers at the regional NCRRR office). These staff will first ensure that a
project and the applicant organization meet basic eligibility criteria as indicated in section 3 above. At
this same time, the ERSF secretariat staff will verify that any comments or concerns raised by the IAF
have been taken into consideration in the project proposal. If a project or organization is found to be
ineligible based on the basic criteria of the ERSF, or if the issues raised by the IAF have not been
addressed or clarified, the proposal will be returned to the proposing organization with a letter explaining
why the project could not be considered for funding.
Once found to be eligible, the Projects and Finance Officer of the regional NCRRR offices will carry out
a general technical review of projects and organizations using the criteria discussed in section 4.1 above.
This review will assess the institutional capacity to carry out the project, the project design, financial
aspects of the project, project sustainability, and the environmental impact of the project. NCRRR has
developed detailed forms that will be used in the application of these criteria to make the review of
project concepts and organizations efficient and objective.
Technical working groups (sub-committee, task force, or ad hoc body) will also conduct a specific
technical assessment of a proposed project. This will be done to ensure that proposals meet standard
technical specifications or norms for the particular sector in which the project is proposed.
The ERSF secretariat will send the proposal to the appropriate government line Ministry or technical
authority to co-ordinate and take responsibility for specific technical reviews of ERSF proposals.
As a part of the overall review process, ERSF secretariat and technical working group members should
visit both the implementing agency's office and the proposed project site to assess and apply the various
review criteria.
The ERSF secretariat will have a maximum of 15 working days from the time a proposal is submitted to
carry out the basic and general technical review of projects. A technical sub-committee will be required
to review and respond to the specific technical issues of a project proposal within 5 days of receipt of the
proposal (or specific technical questions) from NCRRR. The sub-committee will review project
proposals to determine their technical viability, propose modifications (if any) and indicate their
recommendation of the project on technical grounds. Member or members of the sub-committee will be
able to visit the site of a project or the offices of an applicant agency in reviewing the proposal. The



Technical Annex                                    Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                   Attachment 11: Page 11 of 13
Environmental Protection Department of the Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Environment (MAFE)
will assist NCRRR with any sub-projects for which environmental concerns require further review and
analysis. NCRRR regional offices will make appropriate resources (e.g. supplies, logistics, trek
allowances, etc.) available to the technical sub-committee members to ensure adequate and timely action
(including MAFE environmental assessments).
Project Approval. Recommendations from both the ERSF secretariat and respective sub-committee will
be consolidated by the Project Officers and passed on to the ERSF Projects Committee. This Committee
will meet fortnightly to review the technical recommendations and decide to either approve or reject a
project proposal, up to a threshold of US$50,000, equivalent. For projects above this threshold, the
regional ERSF Projects Committee may either reject the project orrecommend a proposal to the central
level for approval. Therefore, a National ERSF Projects Committee will be formed and be given the
authority to approve projects over US$50,000. This committee will be composed of the NCRRR Director
of RRR (chair); the Director of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation; the Director of the SAPA Program;
one representative from the Development Department of MOFDEP; and one representative from
UN/OCHA.
NCRRR will be managing different sources of funding from different donor agencies. Nonetheless, it is
hoped that the procedures presented here can be applied to the greatest extent possible to all outside
funding. However, early in the approval process, the Commission will determine which funding
mechanism at its disposal would be the most appropriate for a given project. By so doing, NCRRR can
then take appropriate internal administrative action to seek any special approvals for different
programmes (such as SAPA) or funding agencies to reduce the need for different external procedures or
requirements of potential implementing partners.
5.3 Implementation Arrangements
Contracting. Once a project has been approved, the ERSF Projects Officer in each region will prepare
the contract for signature based on a standard contract format and required attachments. The contract will
include a summary description of the project, a detailed implementation plan, project budget and
disbursement schedule, and customized forms for physical and financial progress reporting. Either the
Commissioner or Deputy Commissioner of NCRRR will sign all contracts.
Disbursements and Financial Control. The Financial Management and Procurement Unit (FMPU) of
the NCRRR will disburse funds to the implementing partners for project activities once the Commission
has authorized the contract. All disbursements will be made based on monthly financial reports, adequate
documentation of claimed expenditures, and periodic requests for payment submitted by implementing
partners to the regional ERSF Finance Officers. Implementing partners will submit monthly reports and
associated documentation to the Finance Officers no latter than 10 days after the close of the preceding
month. These reports and requests for payment will be reviewed and consolidated at regional level and
sent to the FMPU for final review and payment processing. The same consolidated report is sent to the
ERSF Projects Committee for monitoring purposes. In the event of any questions or concerns raised by
the regional Finance Officers, the Projects Committee, and/or the FMPU, NCRRR staffs will follow-up
directly with the implementing partners.
All financial reporting and disbursement requests will be carried out per the terms of contract between the
Commission and the implementing partners, and all forms for such transactions will be provided to the
implementing partners as attachments to the standard contract. Unless issues or questions arise regarding
reported expenditures and the status of outstanding balances, payments will be made to implementing
partners within 20 days of financial report submission and request for payment.

NCRRR will require that all projects valued at more than $50,000, equivalent, be audited by an
independent public accounting firm. The audit should include a review of the contractual obligations and
restrictions for the ERSF project. If an implementing partner already conducts annual external audits of
its project activities, then this audit will be adequate for NCRRR and ERSF purposes, provided that it also
reviews the contractual obligations of the implementing partner. The incremental costs for such audit will
be an allowable expense under the ERSF project activity. If a project is valued at less than $50,000,
equivalent, and if the implementing partner does not have an independent audit of their accounts
conducted annually, NCRRR will require that all original vouchers for all expenses claimed under ERSF
be submitted to NCRRR as part of the monthly financial report. Otherwise, copies of invoices and
vouchers should be presented with the financial reports.
To further guarantee adequate financial control, the Internal Control Unit of the FMPU will be authorized
to carry out spot checks of ERSF project expenditures reported by implementing partners. Finally, the
semi-annual audits of the overall NCRRR operations will also include a random testing of ERSF project
activities and expenditures.
Monitoring and Reporting. Implementing partners will be required to submit monthly technical reports
to NCRRR that quantify project outputs and quarterly narrative progress reports providing details of
project progress and constraints. Two copies of each report will be submitted to NCRRR; one will go to
the District Supervisor where the project activities are being implemented, and one will go to the
Regional Co-ordinator for the appropriate region. As with the financial reports, these technical progress
reports will be submitted to NCRRR within 10 days of the close of the reporting period. Customized
formats for reporting technical progress will be designed and made available to implementing partners as
attachments to the standard ERSF project contract.



Technical Annex                                         Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                         Attachment 11: Page 13 of 13
District Supervisors will receive the reports and be responsible for verifying the information presented
through regular communications with the implementing partners and field visits to the project sites. The
project officers at regional level will review and consolidate the progress reports for all active projects in
the region. The regional office will have responsibility for entering physical progress data into a
computerized monitoring system. A consolidated report on all regional projects will be prepared by the
project officer and presented to the Projects Committee for review and discussion. A copy of the same
report will be sent to the relevant line ministries in the region. The consolidated report and the electronic
data will also be sent to the Directorate of Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation at the central-level
Commission for further consolidation and analysis. The M&E unit at the central Commission will
prepare a quarterly progress report on the overall implementation of the ERSF.
5.4 Public Sector Institutions
The relevant technical departments, regional, and district offices of the line ministries of the GOSL have
an important role to fulfil in the implementation of the ERSF. First, relevant line ministries (i.e., health,
education, agriculture, works, etc.) are all potential implementing partners to the ERSF. The Commission
fully expects that the appropriate technical units and offices of these ministries will be active participants
in the development and implementation of projects. NCRRR expects to have certain core support
resources available to assist regional and district ministry offices fully participate in the ERSF. Secondly,
the technical ministries will play a critical role at the regional level in providing guidance and input to the
ERSF project staff and Projects Committee.  NCRRR  recognizes the technical expertise and
responsibility that the line ministries have in guaranteeing adherence to sectoral standards and norms.
Therefore, technical sub-committees at the regional level (many that already exist) will be called upon to
provide specific technical know-how and advise on given project proposals. The senior technical officer
from the relevant line ministry or parastatal body will be the contact person for the NCRRR in order to
co-ordinate appropriate technical reviews of ERSF project proposals. NCRRR will make available to
sub-committee members or specified technical personnel in the regions adequate logistical support to
facilitate these technical reviews of proposals. Finally, NCRRR will rely on the line ministry departments
to help monitor project implementation-- especially regarding technical specifications and norms.
District level NCRRR staff responsible for monitoring ERSF project implementation will be responsible
for securing the close co-ordination of line ministry personnel at the same level when specific technical
matters of project implementation are being considered.
Given the special nature of the line ministry departments as the co-ordinator of all national activities for
their respective sectors, and as the regulating body in terms of technical norms and procedures, the
process whereby the line ministries apply to and are financed under the ERSF will vary slightly. First,
any project submitted by a district or regional line ministry office must be endorsed by the appropriate
central ministry department. Second, in the event of a national level project activity (such as for an
information campaign, staff training, etc.), the proposal to the ERSF would be submitted directly to
NCRRR at the central level, and would be reviewed by the National ERSF Projects Committee. Third,
fund disbursement for activities implemented through line ministry departments would have to be
consistent with the financial management requirements of the Government, as well as abide by the
procedures of the FMPU.
Any and all questions regarding the ERSF should be directed to the nearest district or regional NCRRR office (in
Bo, Makeni, Kenema, or the district headquarters towns). Those organizations based in Freetown may seek
information on the ERSF from the central office of the Commission (39 Siaka Stevens Street, tel: 226032) however,
proposals should be submitted through the office nearest to the point where the project would be implemented.

TERMS OF REFERENCE
Assessment of the Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation Project
Pilot Phase
Background
The Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation Project (CRRP) and the related Emergency Recovery
Support Fund (ERSF), and Training and Employment Program (TEP) are Government of Sierra Leone
initiatives co-ordinated by the National Commission for Reconstruction, Resettlement and Rehabilitation
(NCRRR) and the National Committee on Disarmament, Demobilization and Reintegration (NCDDR) to
help address the negative social and economic affects of more than seven years of civil war and ten
months of junta rule. The Commission and Committee have been charged with the responsibility of
helping rebuild the country and will do so in collaboration with key donor and implementing partner
organisations. Several donor organisations, including the World Bank, the African Development Bank,
and the UNDP, support the efforts of these Government institutions to address the urgent needs of
resettling displaced people and refugees, restoring basic social services and infrastructure in the war
affected areas, reintegrating ex-combatants, and addressing the needs of peace building and
reconciliation. The CRRP also supports labour-intensive public works activities designed to assist the
most vulnerable members of society.
The CRRP finances community based sub-projects which address the priority needs of internally
displaced persons, refugees, former combatants, and other vulnerable groups in both rural and urban
areas throughout the country. These sub-projects address multi-sectoral needs; starting with more urgent
food production and temporary shelter, and running through medium and longer term concerns for basic
infrastructure repair and rehabilitation of productive assets. The sub-projects are implemented by eligible
partners which include international and national NGOs, government line ministries, existing donor
project units, and qualified community based organisations. As the financial resources available through
CRRP can only begin to support the long process of rebuilding the country; the Project also looks to
catalysing the self-help efforts of each village and community.
The Project and the ERSF have been designed through a process of consultation, investigation, and
consensus-building initiated by the earlier Ministry of National Reconstruction, Rehabilitation and
Resettlement in mid-1996. The ERSF relies on a decentralised bottom-up project planning, approval and
monitoring process which taps into and builds upon existing systems and structures at the regional and
local levels. NGOs, line ministries and other entities develop project proposals in close collaboration
with village, section and chiefdom authorities. Regional NCRRR staff have primary responsibility for
day-to-day management of the project review process. Regional inter-agency committees have been
formed and given the responsibility to approve projects up to a maximum given level. NCRRR
headquarters' staff provide the broad policy and programme planning framework for the ERSF, monitor
its implementation and co-ordinate the overall process.
In anticipation of an IDA credit worth more than US$20 million, the World Bank is supporting NCRRR
and the ERSF to carry out pilot activities during latter 1998 and early 1999. More than 20 projects worth
almost US$1 million have been reviewed and approved, and have begun implementation under the
Bank's Project Preparation Facility. In light of the innovative nature of the Fund, and the high degree of
inter-agency collaboration and co-operation called for in it implementation, the Government and the
World Bank have decided that a detailed review of the ERSF is required before larger-scale
implementation begins. These terms of reference are for that review.



Technical Annex                                       Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                        Attachment 12: Page 2 of 2
Overall Objective
Improve the effectiveness of the project review, approval, contracting and implementation of the GOSL's
Emergency Recovery Support Fund.
Survey Objectives
A. Determine if the community-based, demand driven strategy of the ERSF has worked to identify and
implement adequate numbers of projects that are of appropriate quality;
B. Determine if any specific type of project, implementing partner, design or implementation strategy
works best (and worst) at achieving the overall goals of the Fund and the National RRR Programme;
C. Assess to what degree approved projects reflect the priorities of a broad cross-section of the
communities in which the projects are implemented.
Specific Tasks and Outputs
1.    Design an assessment strategy and tool(s) to evaluate the implementation of the ERSF that would
be reviewed by NCRRR.
2.    As appropriate, identify local partners and enter into agreement to assist with the implementation
of the assessment.
3.    Review relevant project documentation; i.e., ERSF Manual, project proposals, review documents,
minutes from project committee meetings, contracts, etc.
4.    Interview implementing partners at national and regional levels with experience in submitting
proposals to the ERSF, implementing ERSF projects, or participated in the project review process on their
experience with the proposal development, review and approval process.
5.    Interview NCRRR staff, ERSF Project Committee members, and technical line ministry
representatives on the project review and approval process.
6.    Evaluate level of community input in project identification, design and implementation.
7.    Assess effectiveness of fund disbursement mechanisms from NCRRR to ERSF implementing
partners.
8.    Assess implementing partners' adherence to contract responsibilities in terms reporting, record
keeping and procurement.
9.    Assess NCRRR's performance in terms of financial and technical record keeping and project
monitoring.
10.   Conduct a workshop involving major stakeholders to the ERSF to discuss findings of the
assessment and to identify possible improvements to the ERSF review, approval and implementation
process.
11.   Prepare a final report to Government within 4 weeks of the conclusion of the assessment
recommending changes or adjustments to the ERSF and outlining a plan of action to put in place the
recommendations.



Technical Annex                                        Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                         Attachment 13: Page I of 4
Social Safety Net Component of the CRRP
It has been agreed with Government that as part of the Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Project (CRRP) a social safety net component would be implemented that would focus on labor-intensive
public works programs. The target beneficiaries of this temporary safety net would be ex-combatants,
unemployed urban youth, and members of recently returned rural communities, and other traditional
vulnerable groups such as female-headed households, etc. The safety net component will be operational
for the same three-year period that the CRRP will be effective.
After careful consideration of worldwide experiences with social safety net schemes, and consultations
with likely implementing partners and Government authorities responsible for relevant aspects of such an
intervention, the following observations and recommendations are made.
Project Design and Selection Criteria
1. Wage rate is the critical determinant of targeting as well as distribution of benefits in favor of poor
participants. In general, a wage that is close to or slightly below the prevailing market wage for
unskilled labor is most likely to encourage self-selection of the poor into the program, to maximize
the amount of temporary employment created under the program. Based on an assessment of current
market practices, related works programs managed by the Sierra Leonean Works Authority, and non-
governmental organizations' food-for-work activities, the prevailing daily rate is approximately
Le.1,500. This finding is roughly consistent with earlier findings of World Bank missions.
Therefore, it is recommended that the wage rate for the CRRP's social safety net component not
exceed this level.
2. Share of wages to total project cost. As the first objective of this component is to provide for
temporary employment opportunities for the identified vulnerable groups, the component should
strive to maximize the labor content of funded projects. Based on both reviews of proposed projects,
and experience in similar countries, the labor component of a projectI should represent
approximately 50% of the total cost. Therefore, a target range of 40 to 70 percent should be
recommended for the CRRP, depending on the type and location of a project. Projects with labor
costs above this range would not ensure adequate quality of the completed projects, and below this
range would not meet the objective of the program.
3. Geographic targeting. In order to make it easiest for the identified vulnerable groups to participate
in the component, and therefore reduce transaction costs, it is recommended that proposed activities
target those areas where there is general agreement that the concentration of poverty is greatest. For
instance, in Freetown such areas include Goderich, Congo Town/Banana Water areas, Dworzak
Farm, Redpump, Kroobay, OdokokolMountain Court, Mabella, Ginger Hall, and many pockets in
Kissy and Wellington. In addition, those rural communities that have recently returned home
(i.e., the majority of the population having arrived within the previous 12 months) should be targeted.
4. Other design features.  Project designs should include other features that facilitate broad
participation; for example, piece-rate or task-based payment, flexible timing of participation (where
possible), provision of day care arrangements, and timing not to conflict with other demands such as
planting season, or the school year for youths.
Labor costs directed at the target group or groups; i.e., excluding supervisory and/or management personnel costs



Technical Annex                                        Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                         Attachment 13: Page 2 of 4
Project Identification and Development
While the CRRP wishes to target those vulnerable groups identified for the social safety net component
of the Project, the overall implementation strategy of the CRRP is to use a community-based demand
driven project identification and development process. This same strategy is advisable for the social
safety net component for consistency sake and also to encourage that the social and/or economic
infrastructure created, rehabilitated or maintained through the public works activity is considered a
priority by the community that will ultimately use it.
Therefore, it is expected that NGOs, line ministries, or public authorities (such as SLRA) will prepare
project proposals through consultation with relevant communities and/or identified vulnerable groups.
By doing so, not only will the priorities of the community be better expressed in the project design, but
the ownership of the end-product greater-- thus encouraging on-going community maintenance of the
asset.
Project proposals must demonstrate that the requisite technical input, standards and/or norms have been
taken into consideration and that appropriate technical supervision will be available for project
implementation. For instance, road rehabilitation or maintenance activities should reflect the SLRA
norms and should cater for adequate monitoring, works rehabilitation proposals should have standard
drawings and bills of quantities attached (if appropriate), works involving sanitation, agriculture or
reforestation should indicate how the relevant line ministries (health and agriculture) have been consulted
in project design and will be involved in implementation.
Project Review and Selection
It is recommended that the proposed ERSF project review and approval procedures overseen by NCRRR
be used for the social safety net component of the CRRP. The same staff and organizational structures
are capable of handling these types of projects, and the inter-agency review process will help guarantee
both technical suitability of projects and coordination with other similar activities (such as food for work
or food for agriculture). Specific technical criteria (indicated below) should be incorporated into the
ERSF Operational Manual and be used in reviewing and assessing these types of public works projects.
Technical review and inter-agency approval structures have been established to assess and select
community based projects that are adequately suited to the task of reviewing labor-intensive works
projects. The recent revisions to the institutional structure of NCRRR that include adding a Western Area
office and seeking civil engineers for district level posts, will facilitate the inclusion of public works
project proposals in the review process. As with the ERSF, NCRRR is the coordinating institution and
financing channel for project activities; the actual implementing agencies will be the partner
organizations to the ERSF; namely NGOs, line ministries, and public authorities.
Specific Review Criteria
*   The component's priority should be to create temporary employment among the most vulnerable,
while at the same time optimizing the social, economic and/or physical infrastructure of the
community and, if possible, be oriented toward further job creation and/or income generation.
*   Project wage rate should not exceed the prevailing market wage for unskilled labor. At present,
the wage rate varies, depending on location. The average daily wage rate for unskilled labor is
approximately Le 1,500. The monthly wage for unskilled labor varies, between Le 35,000 and Le
45,000 (for 25 working days).
*   The total wage component for unskilled labor of the public works project should be equal to or
exceed 50 percent of the total project cost, on average.



Technical Annex                                      Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                       Attachment 13: Page 3 of 4
Depending on the type of public works and the location, wages could be paid in both cash and in-
kind (i.e., food), however, the total value should not exceed a daily wage rate of Le.1, 500 as
discussed above. Discussions between SLRA and NGOs, for instance, suggest that for the
implementation of all food-aid projects, 30% of the labor cost shall be paid in food and 70% in
cash.
*   The project encourages to create linkages between the proposed public works schemes and the
productivity with which the works are carried out, through incorporating a piece-rate or task-
based payment system for laborers. (SLRA's Feeder Roads Department, for instance, has
established wage rates per man-day for 18 different road rehabilitation activities).
*  Proposed public works projects need to be consistent with existing labor laws.
*   Proposed public works projects need to be technically feasible and proposals must demonstrate
that appropriate technical norms and design standards will be adhered to.
*   Targeted communities should participate in project design and the services or works to be
delivered should respond to the needs of these groups.
*   Timing and design of services/works should, to the extent possible, encourage broadest
participation and reduce opportunity costs of participation through seasonal timing (i.e., not
during the planting or harvesting seasons) and flexible working arrangements (such as piece meal
work).
*   The number of laborers at any one-job site should range between 50 and 75, and should not
exceed 100.
*   During the pilot phase of the ERSF, it is not recommended that any one public works project
exceed $150,000 in value.
*   The implementing partners proposing the public works projects need to ensure a reliable and
regular flow of funds for the payment of workers and put in place a financial management system
that promotes the expedient implementation of the works.
*   To the extent possible, proposed public works programs should be targeting communities where
there is general agreement that the concentration of poverty is greatest. In the initial phase of the
project, the identification of the poor will be based on geographic targeting. Priority will be given
to pockets of poor in urban areas and recently returned communities in rural areas. The needs of
particular vulnerable groups, such as ex-combatants, unemployed youth or female-headed
households, will be addressed through these broader geographically targeted programs.
Financing Modalities
The same financing procedures proposed for the overall ERSF should also apply to the social safety net
component of the CRRP. Namely, once a contract with an implementing partner is authorized by
NCRRR, the Financial Management and Procurement Unit will manage all financial disbursements to the
organization. The FMPU will be established as an autonomous unit reporting to NCRRR but which will
operate independently from normnal fund disbursement procedures of the public sector. Financial staff of
NCRRR at regional levels and in the Western Area will have responsibility for reviewing monthly
financial reports from implementing partners. It will be the responsibility of the implementing agencies
to guarantee timely disbursement of wages to laborers.



Technical Annex                                      Community Reintegration and Rehabilitation
Sierra Leone                                                      Attachment 13: Page 4 of 4
Implementation Mechanisms
The mission met with various Government agencies and NGO organizations and was able to confirm the
conclusion of previous World Bank missions that considerable capacity exists in Sierra Leone, both in the
private sector and in the local and international NGO community, to carry out labor-intensive public
works programs. Since a recent CRRP mission, in May'98, several public works activities have been
initiated or are being prepared. WFP implementing partners alone will employ 78,000 people for food-
for-agriculture activities over a five-month period from May to September of the current year. NGOs like
CARE, CRS, and World Vision have also been implementing food-for-a.griculture programs during the
ongoing rainy season and are preparing food-for-work programs for the upcoming dry season. Certain
organizations are also contemplating cash-for-work programs. During recent discussions between the
government (SLRA) and some of its implementing partners (NGOs), agreement was reached that for the
implementation of all food-aid projects, 30% of the labor cost shall be paid in food and 70% in cash.
In addition, the Government is encouraging linkages between the proposed public works schemes and the
productivity with which the works are carried out. In particular, through incorporating a piece-rate or
task-based payment system for laborers. SLRA's Feeder Roads Department, for instance, has established
piece rates per man-day for 18 different road rehabilitation activities. To improve implementation
capacity in Moyamba, Bonthe and Pujehan districts, SLRA field staff and several small-scale contractors
are currently involved in refresher courses and skills upgrading training on emergency spot improvement
of feeder roads and bridges. The Ministry of Youth has prepared several public works proposals for urban
youth, and Civil Development Units have continued to mobilize urban youth to do voluntary work such
as garbage cleaning. These developments indicate a growing capacity to plan and implement public
works programs in both urban as well as rural Sierra Leone. The mission is of the opinion that for the
implementation of its Social Safety Net component, the CRRP can largely rely on this increasing
capacity.
Monitoring and Evaluation
The established procedures and mechanisms in place for monitoring the ERSF will be used to monitor the
labor-intensive works activities.  Specifically, district level staff (supervisors and community
development officers) and the Western Area manager and project officer will monitor specific indicators
of progress. These will include timely payment of wages, level of employment created (man-days),
timely completion of works, and quality of works. In assessing the latter two aspects of the physical
works carried out, NCRRR staff will seek the assistance of the Government line ministries and public
authorities as necessary.
